==PAGE:1==
 
 
 
 
 
MAPPING VALUES  
IN THE PROCESS OF 
SUSTAINABLE  
DWELLING RENOVATION 
 
 
 
 
 
A CASE STUDY 
February 2017 
Thesis author 
Stephanie Salling 
Thesis advisors 
Associate Professor Christian Thuesen, Technical University of Denmark 
PhD MSc. ENG Søren Wandahl, Aarhus University School of Engineering 
==PAGE:2==
 
[ii] 
 
ABSTRACT 
The observed climate changes in recent years are “extremely likely” to have been 
caused mainly by anthropogenic emissions. Only by continually and substantially 
reducing emissions is it possible to limit the risks of further climate changes.  
The construction industry plays a central role in achieving climate goals, because 1) 
there is a major potential for energy savings in existing buildings, and 2) a spate of 
renovations is imminent. This justifies researching whether there is a potential for 
improvement in the renovation process, and what this improvement could be.  
Renovation projects are acknowledged to be far more complex than new building, 
resulting in a low productivity in renovation. Furthermore, some do not believe that the 
potential to include sustainability in renovations is currently fully reached, and despite 
previous efforts, there is no indication that the renovation process has improved. Two 
recently launched research projects, ReVALUE and REBUS, strive to change this. An 
external aim of this thesis is to contribute to the two projects. 
The objectives of this thesis are based on the wish to maximize value creation for all 
stakeholders in future sustainable dwelling renovations. Case study research is used as the 
methodological framework for collecting data, and the main case is the social housing 
department Åbyhøjgård.  
Firstly, the primary stakeholders in the main case are determined through a 
stakeholder analysis. On this basis, 17 people are chosen as interview respondents. Their 
internal dynamics are examined through a Social Network Map, which reveals a well-
integrated network of communication.  
Secondly, the Value Proposition Canvas, which is developed for the retail industry, 
is used to form interview questions. This can facilitate revealing important aspects in the 
renovation process seen by the key stakeholders, in the form of gains and pains, and in this 
way conceptualize the term value creation. Three additional respondents, who represent 
two social housing organizations managing two other cases, have been interviewed to 
increase the validity and appropriateness for generalization of the findings. 11 gains and 
10 pains are identified, some of which influence each other. Finally, an investigation of 
whether agreements on the important topics exist between stakeholders shows a varying 
degree of agreement.  
Additional to answering the research questions, this research has led to two 
contributions to current theory. One is the use of the Value Proposition Canvas in a new 
context, where the “customers” are all the different stakeholders in a construction project. 
The other is the use of the Value Proposition Canvas in combination with Social Network 
Analysis. This combination enables the Social Network Map to also deal with the context 
of exchanged information, not only the presence or absence of links. 
 
==PAGE:3==
 
[iii] 
 
DANSK RESUME 
De seneste års observerede klimaforandringer skyldes "højst sandsynligt" primært 
menneskeskabte emissioner. Kun ved væsentligt og kontinuerligt at mindske emissionerne 
i fremtiden, er det muligt at begrænse risikoen for yderligere klimaforandringer. 
Byggeindustrien spiller en central rolle i at nå de fastsatte klimamål, fordi 1) der er 
et stort potentiale for energibesparelser i eksisterende bygninger, og 2) en omfattende bølge 
af renoveringer er nært forestående. Dette gør det relevant at undersøge, om der er et 
potentiale for at forbedre den nuværende renoveringsproces, og hvad dette potentiale 
kunne være. 
Det er anerkendt, at renoveringsprojekter er mere komplekse end nybyggeri, og at 
dette resulterer i en lav produktivitet i renovering. Desuden er der flere, som ikke tror, 
potentialet for at inkludere bæredygtighed i renoveringer er udnyttet fuldt ud, og trods 
tidligere indsatser, er der ingen tegn på, at renoveringsprocessen er forbedret. To nyligt 
lancerede forskningsprojekter, ReVALUE og REBUS, stræber efter at ændre dette. Et 
eksternt formål med denne afhandling er at bidrage til de to projekter. 
Formålene i denne afhandling er baseret på et ønske om at maksimere værdi-
skabelsen for alle interessenter i fremtidens  energirenoveringer af boliger. Case Study 
research anvendes som metodologisk ramme for indsamling af data, og hovedcasen er den 
almene boligafdeling Åbyhøjgård. 
Først bestemmes de primære interessenter i hovedcasen gennem en interessent-
analyse. På dette grundlag er 17 personer valgt som interview-respondenter. Deres interne 
dynamik undersøges gennem et socialt netværkskort (Social Network Map), som viser et 
velintegreret netværk af kommunikation. 
Dernæst anvendes Value Proposition Canvas, som er kendt fra detailhandlen, til at 
formulere interviewspørgsmål. Disse kan klarlægge hvilke aspekter, de centrale interes-
senter ser som vigtige i renoveringsprocessen, i form af gains og pains, og på den måde 
konceptualisere begrebet værdiskabelse. Tre yderligere respondenter, der repræsenterer 
to almene boligorganisationer, som administrerer to andre renoveringer, er blevet 
interviewet for at øge gyldigheden og generaliseringsfaktoren af resultaterne. 11 gains og 
10 pains er identificeret, hvoraf nogle påvirker hinanden indbyrdes. Til sidst undersøges 
det, om der er enighed mellem interessenterne om, hvilke emner, der er vigtige. Det viser 
sig, at der er en varierende grad af enighed blandt respondenterne. 
Ud over at besvare formålene, har denne forskning ført til to bidrag til nuværende 
teori. Den ene er anvendelsen af Value Proposition Canvas i en ny sammenhæng, hvor 
”kunderne” er alle de forskellige interessenter i et byggeprojekt. Den anden er anvendelsen 
af Value Proposition Canvas i kombination med social netværksanalyse. Denne kombi-
nation gør det muligt for det sociale netværkskort også at beskæftige sig med indholdet af 
udvekslet information, ikke kun om forbindelser eksisterer eller ej.

GLOSSARY AND ABBREVIATIONS 
The kind of social housing that exists in Denmark is rather unique, so the translation 
of the Danish terminology might not be completely accurate.  Thus, to avoid 
misunderstandings, some key expressions used in the paper are explained here, with 
inspiration from The Danish National Building Fund (DNBF n.d.). Moreover, 
abbreviations have been created for a number of expressions that are used repeatedly 
throughout the report. They are also listed here. The list is structured according to topic, 
not alphabetically. 
Abbreviation: Abbrev. This abbreviation is used to enable the reader to distinguish 
abbreviations from references, as both are shown in brackets throughout the report. 
Social housing (in Danish: Almene boliger): This term covers three different types 
of housing; social family dwellings, social dwellings for the elderly and social 
dwellings for young people. The Danish social housing sector comprises of around 
595,000 dwellings, which makes up about 20% of the total housing stock in Denmark. 
The three types of social housing are distributed as follows: 82% of the social housing 
stock are family dwellings, 12% are dwellings for the elderly and 6% are dwellings 
for young people. Social housing is constructed and run by social housing 
organizations. 
Social housing organizations: There are approximately 700 social housing 
organizations with a total of around 7,500 divisions (departments), which are all run 
on a non-profit basis. Their main objectives are to construct, rent out, manage, 
maintain and modernize social dwellings. The social housing organizations are 
subject to municipal supervision. 
Resident democracy: The resident democracy in social housing is rather complex, 
and unique to the Danish social housing system. Residents choose, by simple 
majority, the residents they want to be in the board of the department. The board can 
make decisions on behalf of the residents. Decisions, that affect all residents, such as 
a sustainable renovation that includes work inside apartments, are out to a vote 
among all residents. 
The Danish National Building Fund (abbrev. DNBF) (in Danish: Landsbygge-
fonden): The fund was established in 1967 with the purpose of providing financial 
support and skilled assistance to social housing organizations. It is financed through 
compulsory rent fees from tenants of departments established before 1970, and 
through payments from repaid loans. The fund grants money to e.g. renovations, 
social and preventive measures, and new building projects. 
General plan (in Danish: Helhedsplan): This term is generally used to describe great 
efforts made in social housing departments, which cover both social, economic, and 
physical aspects of renewing a department. The general plans are often initiated 
==PAGE:10==
 
[x] 
 
based on undesirable high rates of residents from the lower stratum, and possibly 
problems with crime and vandalism in the area. However, the term “general plan” 
as such merely means a comprehensive plan for renovation. 
The Value Proposition Canvas (abbrev. VPC): A theoretical tool used in the retail 
industry to ensure the best possible match between product and customer (see 
explanation in chapter 5.2). The Value Proposition Canvas is used in this thesis as a 
tool to conceptualize the rather abstract term of value creation. It is used to develop 
interview questions that can reveal value-adding and value inhibiting aspects of the 
renovation process. 
Gains: A term from the VPC. It covers positive outcomes and benefits of products, 
which the customers either require, expect, desire, or would be surprised by. Gains 
include positive emotions, cost savings, functional utility, and social gains 
(Strategyzer 2014). In other words, gains create value for the customer. 
Pains: A term from the VPC. It covers all negative aspects that the customers would 
like to avoid. Pains could be negative emotions, costs or risks, and they can have 
different degrees of importance to the customer (Strategyzer 2014). In other words, 
pains inhibit value creation for the customer. 
Social Network Analysis (abbrev. SNA): A theory within social science, explained 
in chapter 5.3. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:11==
Introduction 
[1] 
 
1 INTRODUCTION 
The climate is changing. The atmosphere and oceans are warming and ice and snow 
are melting, causing sea levels to rise. The changes in climate have already had impacts on 
natural and human systems around the globe (IPCC 2014). Human influence on the climate 
system is also unambiguous. Especially anthropogenic emissions of greenhouse gases such 
as CO2 are ‘extremely likely’ to have been the main cause of the observed warming in the 
past 60 years (IPCC 2014). Only by continually and substantially reducing emissions of 
greenhouse gases in the future, is it possible to limit the risks, further changes in the climate 
would cause, including irreversible and severe impacts for both people and ecosystems 
(IPCC 2014). Thus, the actions taken now to reduce emissions may be of crucial importance 
to limit the long-term global warming (Alsbjørn et al. 2008). 
Persistently reducing resource- and energy consumption demands a change of 
processes in all sectors of society, especially in the industrialized countries, such as 
Denmark. During the past two decades, several multinational protocols have been passed 
by the countries under the United Nation’s Framework Convention on Climate Change 
(abbrev. UNFCCC), committing the members to take actions to minimize climate change. 
Most recently, the Paris Agreement was signed by more than 180 countries, committing to 
reductions that cover more than 96% of total emissions, compared to 15% in the foregoing 
Kyoto Agreement (Klimaministeriet 2015). This protocol took effect from November 4th 
2016 (Energi 2016).  
To accomplish the goal set in the Paris Agreement of keeping global warming below 
an average of 2°C, it is important to look at both potential energy savings, energy efficiency 
improvements and conversion to renewable energy sources (Alsbjørn et al. 2008). These 
three aspects are closely connected; by exploiting possible energy savings, energy 
efficiency can be improved, and the prospect of renewable energy sources covering the 
production will become brighter. 
1.1 PROBLEM DEFINITION 
In Denmark – as in most other European countries – the construction industry is 
responsible for 40% of energy consumption and 1/3 of the total waste production 
(Energistyrelsen 2014; InnoByg 2016). Hence, this sector plays a central role when it comes 
to achieving climate goals. 
Most of the buildings that will be standing in 30-40 years have already been built, 
and analyses by the International Energy Agency show a major potential for energy 
savings in existing buildings (Energistyrelsen 2014). Therefore, sustainable measures are 
of utmost importance in renovation in the near future. In addition, it is estimated that nine 
out of ten properties in the EU are to be renovated within the next few decades to meet the 
Union’s ambitions for energy savings (Aarhus Universitet 2014). An efficient renovation 
==PAGE:12==
Introduction 
[2] 
 
process – in terms of both time, energy, and resource consumption – is necessary to ensure 
overall energy savings. 
The imminent spate of renovations in the EU as well as the rest of the world (Attalla 
& Hegazy 2003; Kemmer & Koskela 2014), makes it worth researching whether renovations 
are currently carried out in the most optimal way possible, or if there is room for 
improvement in some aspects of the process. 
In Denmark, a boom in construction was seen after the Second World War. A huge 
shortage of housing led to government imposed regulations, stating that dwellings built 
with concrete elements – a very time-efficient way of building – would receive financial 
support. As a result, social housing projects were being constructed in unprecedented 
speed with around 600,000 dwellings erected during the years 1960-1979 (Bech-Danielsen 
et al. 2014). Within the last decade or so, renovation of these social housing dwellings has 
begun. This is mainly owing to structural elements needing repair or replacement, but also 
because buildings from this period have a specifically major potential for energy savings 
(Bech-Danielsen et al. 2011), which should be exploited in order to reach governmental 
sustainable targets.  
Due to the large extent to which social housing projects will be subjects of renovation 
in Denmark in the years to come, this type of projects is suitable as subject for research 
regarding improvements of the renovation process.  
 
 
==PAGE:13==
State of the art 
[3] 
 
2 STATE OF THE ART 
This chapter will introduce the state of the art in renovation processes. First, the 
complexity of renovation projects compared to new building is explained. Then, a 
clarification of the current shortcomings in implementing sustainable aspects in 
renovations leads to a description of a newly developed tool, called RENO-EVALUE. 
Subsequently, views from existing literature on user involvement, and how it can create 
value in construction projects are elucidated, followed by case examples of implementing 
user involvement. Finally, current initiatives on the topics of improving the renovation 
process are presented.  
2.1 MANAGING RENOVATION PROJECTS 
The management process of constructing new buildings is a well-documented area, 
and – compared to renovation projects – rather straight forward. So far, the topic of 
managing renovation projects has not enjoyed the same attention (Kemmer & Koskela 
2012). Maybe this is because it was not until recently that the industry started looking at 
renovation as something different to new building (Værdibyg 2013). However, due to e.g. 
space-constraints, higher risks, poor documentation of existing structures, and occupation 
of buildings, renovation is a much more complex task than new building, according to 
several articles (Krizek et al. 1996; Egbu 1997; Kemmer & Koskela 2012). The constraints 
and barriers in renovations are summarized in Table 2-1. 
Constraints and barriers in renovation 
Constraints of space 
Higher risks 
Poor documentation of existing structures 
Occupation of buildings 
Uniqueness of projects 
Table 2-1 Constraints and barriers in renovation 
Krizek et al. elaborate on the reasons for the complexity; constraints of space include 
existing plumbing and electrical systems; higher risks are caused by the limited access and 
access time; frequent inconsistencies between drawings and reality; and a great number of 
involved parties – including users or residents (Krizek et al. 1996). The large number of 
involved parties requires effective communication and interaction among all stakeholders 
for the project to be successful. The view of Krizek is backed by Attalla and Hegazy, who 
have identified some of the main factors that contribute to the success of a large scale 
renovation as early formation of a good plan for communication among involved parties 
and a practical site layout plan. They also learned that two important reasons for delays 
and cost overruns were unforeseen site conditions, and late scope and design changes 
(Attalla & Hegazy 2003).  
One of the barriers to improve productivity in construction in general, is the 
uniqueness that characterizes construction projects. The variance is even bigger between – 
==PAGE:14==
State of the art 
[4] 
 
and also within – renovation projects. Plenty obscure details always emerge during the 
process, which leaves the manager with a feeling of moving “two steps forward, one step 
back” (Byggeproces 2013 p. 6). Accordingly, research shows a generally lower performance 
of renovation projects than of new building (McKim et al. 2000).  
Though stated in (Værdibyg 2013) that renovation was just recently recognized by 
the industry as being different from rebuilding – at least in Denmark – British professor 
Charles Egbu foresaw the need for specific education for construction managers within 
renovation almost two decades ago (Egbu 1997). According to Egbu, the growth in the 
renovation market already at this time made it relevant to look at the future challenges and 
opportunities within renovation management, in order to identify the needs for training 
and education. Both the managers and the organizations interviewed in his research found 
the two most important fields, where education was needed, to be ‘health and safety’ and 
‘quality control and assurance’. The organizations furthermore ranked ‘public 
relations/tenant liaison’ as the 7th most important field for future training and education. 
This aligns well with the constraints mentioned in the above paragraphs and in Table 2-1.  
Egbu’s findings are highly relevant today, and the topic of special education needs 
for renovation managers is not being overlooked. Since 2011, the initiative “Renovering på 
dagsordenen” (in English: ”Renovation on the agenda”) has sought to increase the focus 
on building renovation among the public, politicians, and the construction industry in 
Denmark (Bygherreforeningen 2015). One of the focal points of the initiative is the supply 
and demand of competences and education within renovation management. Surveys, 
analyses, and meetings have shown an ever increasing effort among institutions to 
incorporate renovation in educations within engineering, to live up to the growing demand 
for these competences in the industry. However, institutions point to the dilemma that to 
make room for more courses in renovation, other topics have to be cut. 
2.2 SUSTAINABLE RENOVATION 
Most (if not all) recent and current renovations in Denmark include sustainable 
aspects, but sustainability is still rarely the main motive for initiating a renovation (Jensen 
& Maslesa 2013). Besides the previously mentioned report conducted for the Danish 
Building Research Institute (abbrev. SBi) (Bech-Danielsen et al. 2011), two reports on this 
topic have been compiled, both by students from Roskilde University; a master’s project 
(Alsbjørn et al. 2008); and a bachelor’s thesis (Raundahl & Mogensen 2010). Both of these 
reports seek to elucidate the big potential for energy savings and sustainable renovation in 
the social housing sector, on the basis that this potential is not currently being reached. 
Correspondingly, Jensen & Maslesa state that opportunities – e.g. to implement 
further sustainable improvements – are often lost in renovation projects due to lack of 
knowledge and insufficient communication between stakeholders.  
In their research carried out for the Technical University of Denmark, Jensen & 
Maslesa identified the need for a simple and holistic tool to support communication 
==PAGE:15==
State of the art 
[5] 
 
regarding the aims of renovation projects. The research resulted in RENO-EVALUE, a 
concrete tool meant to be used as both a support in decision making and to evaluate 
renovation projects (Jensen & Maslesa 2015). The tool’s results take the form of a spider 
web graph (see Figure 2-1), which illustrates the importance of a range of aspects to different 
stakeholders – primarily in the early stages of renovation projects. The aim is that the tool 
should be implemented in future projects, so that expectations are aligned and issues can 
be dealt with in an early stage to the satisfaction of all parties.  
 
Figure 2-1 Example of a RENO-EVALUE evaluation (Jensen & Maslesa 2015) 
The aim of RENO-EVALUE is very similar to the one of the current research, but the 
focus of RENO-EVALUE is slightly different. RENO-EVALUE focuses mainly on the 
outcome of the renovation and the related expectations, whereas this thesis investigates 
the values in the renovation process itself. Nevertheless, the RENO-EVALUE tool is a 
source of inspiration. 
2.3 USER INVOLVEMENT 
As stated above, several researchers have found communication to be of very high 
importance in renovation projects (Attalla & Hegazy 2003; Jensen & Maslesa 2015). One 
aspect of the communication is user involvement. According to (Værdibyg 2012), user 
involvement is of fundamental importance for any construction project to be successful. 
The special circumstances of renovation projects – such as residents living on site during 
construction – make handling and involving users especially important in these projects. 
Several studies have been carried out about stakeholder involvement in construction 
in general, and specifically about the benefits early stakeholder involvement provides 
(McKim et al. 2000; Løssing & Delman 2005; Bertelsen 2002) in particular to value creation 
(Aapaoja et al. 2013). Involving especially the users of the buildings undergoing renovation 
can contribute to a feeling of ownership, which can be a significant advantage towards 
ensuring a smooth construction process (Værdibyg 2012). 
==PAGE:16==
State of the art 
[6] 
 
As mentioned, users can create value in a project, but they can also create challenges, 
and planning of the communication with users should be done as early in the process as 
possible to achieve the best result. This is also the opinion stated in the guide “Håndtering 
af brugere og beboere i renoveringsprocessen” (in English: ”Handling of users and 
residents in the renovation process”), which focuses entirely on the importance of 
communication with users/residents during a renovation (Værdibyg 2015).  
Værdibyg has developed a wide-ranging set of recommendations and guidelines to 
help smoothen the construction process in general. “Håndtering af brugere og beboere i 
renoveringsprocessen” belongs to a subset of guidelines particularly aiming at the 
renovation process. The guide explains how to identify the users and categorize them as 
“should be informed”, “should be briefed”, “should be heard”, and “should be involved” 
(Værdibyg 2015, p. 6), see Figure 2-2. It then elaborates on what, when, and how to 
communicate, and who should be handling the communication with users and residents.  
 
Figure 2-2 Categorization of users. Inspiration from (Værdibyg 2015) 
Another publication from Værdibyg, “Perspektiver på renoveringsprocesser”, is 
looking at different perspectives on the renovation process (Værdibyg 2013). It explains 
the concept of “the experienced value” as an important factor for the success of a 
renovation project. The experienced value is closely related to both the course of the project 
and how the result appears. With maybe only 10% of the work carried out during a 
renovation being visible at the end of the project, the process is of great importance to the 
perception of the quality of the result (Værdibyg 2013). This implies a possible advantage 
in involving users not only in the decisions about the outcome of a renovation, but also 
about the renovation process itself.  
Unfortunately, the renovation process does not always run as smoothly as desired, 
e.g. due to the previously mentioned constraints and barriers (Table 2-1). Aapaoja et al. 
mention ‘misalignment in expectations’ among the involved stakeholders as a root cause 
of misunderstandings during construction (Aapaoja et al. 2013). This adds to the relevance 
==PAGE:17==
State of the art 
[7] 
 
of the above explained RENO-EVALUE tool. The misunderstandings can cause 
complications that can be devastating to the project. Thus, disputes should be worked out 
as early in the process as possible to avoid complications in a later phase of the project. 
According to the findings of McKim et al. (McKim et al. 2000), complications that can be 
eliminated through early user involvement include interruptions of the construction 
operations, indicating that money and time can be saved by handling users appropriately. 
 CASES 
As stated, many advocate the positive effect user involvement has on both the 
construction process and the perception of the result. However, documentation of concrete 
implementation of user involvement strategies in construction projects is scarce. Within 
the timeframe of this research, no documentation of application in renovation projects in 
specific has been obtained, only of application in new buildings.  
The user involvement in the documentation found addresses the outcome of 
construction projects, not the construction process itself. This may be because the users are 
not affected to the same degree by the construction process of new buildings (since they 
are not on the construction site during the process), as they are in renovations. Yet, the 
cases still illustrate the added value and more positive perception of the result, user 
involvement can facilitate, as described in the above paragraphs. 
The following example shows both the problems that can arise if user involvement 
is neglected, and the benefits of proper user involvement. In a publication from 2006, prof. 
Per Anker Jensen describes, among other things, the user involvement in the infamous 
construction of the new headquarter for the Danish Broadcasting Corporation (Danmarks 
Radio) (Jensen 2006). In the beginning of the process, only the presidents of the unions 
were involved in forming the demands and wishes for the new headquarter; other users 
were not involved at all. When the completed building program was presented to the users, 
it resulted in massive protests, which – after months of negotiation – led to a reevaluation 
of the program. A group was established to propose new models and procedures for 
further user involvement. They appointed a number of working groups within different 
areas of work, each consisting of around 10 people, who should then communicate the 
demands and wishes of the users within this field. Around 20 working groups were 
appointed. Among other things, they contributed with detailed descriptions of future work 
functions as well as area needs, and they commented on both the winning project and the 
later project material in the disposition - and project proposal phase. The user involvement 
in the programming phase went well. Most involved users were very engaged in the 
project, and their view on the project changed in a positive direction. Later, when doing 
evaluation interviews, Jensen found that users were satisfied with the user involvement, 
and that it gave them a greater feeling of ownership of the result (Jensen 2007), exactly as 
was argued a couple of years later in (Værdibyg 2012). Furthermore, respondents stated 
that the user involvement led to the building better fitting their needs, and to more 
motivated personnel. 
==PAGE:18==
State of the art 
[8] 
 
In two other examples, manual and digital visualization have been successfully used 
as a part of user involvement in the early design phase of a medical office construction 
project, and a participatory community planning project, respectively (Leicht & Kumar 
2010; Al-Kodmany 1999). In the medical office project, a virtual mock-up of the entire 
building was reviewed by both end-users, the future facility manager and members of the 
project team. They pointed out weaknesses and possible improvements, which were then 
implemented. Afterwards, a survey was conducted among the participants in the mock-
up review to assess their experience with the review session. The participants were all very 
satisfied with the review. They felt it improved their understanding of and confidence in 
the project. Some also pointed out the value of having the full range of team members 
present, since this allowed for any issue raised to be solved on the spot. (Leicht & Kumar 
2010). 
In the other example, the participatory community planning project, manual 
sketching by an artist was combined with use of maps from a GIS (Geographic Information 
System), and photo-manipulation. The two first-mentioned methods were used in the 
initial design workshops, and photo-manipulation was used in later work-shops. Distrust 
in the community was the background for the participatory, collaborative approach to the 
planning and design of a revitalization of a commercial neighborhood. The researchers 
found that the three methods empowered the residents to plan and design for the future 
of their community (Al-Kodmany 1999). 
2.4 SUMMARY 
In summary, renovation projects are acknowledged to be far more complex than new 
building due to numerous constraints. These together with the great variance between 
renovation projects result in low productivity in renovation. It is recognized that due to 
the complexity, special competences are needed of managers of renovation projects, and 
the growth of the renovation market means the subject is being incorporated increasingly 
in relevant educations.  
Several publications conclude that there is a great potential for energy savings in 
renovation of public housing, yet some do not believe that the potential to include 
sustainability in renovations is currently fully reached. To ensure that no sustainable 
opportunities are missed, the tool RENO-EVALUE has been developed. It can facilitate the 
decision making process in renovation through alignment of expectations, primarily of the 
result, but also of the process. RENO-EVALUE takes account of all stakeholders, including 
the users of the subject of renovation. User involvement is crucial for a renovation project 
to be successful, and it can facilitate value creation for users/residents. Værdibyg has 
developed some guidelines on user involvement to equip renovation managers for the 
process. Concrete case-strategies and implementation of user involvement is a scarcely 
documented area, and consequently, the documentation obtained only concerns new 
building. In this documentation, user involvement has only been addressing the outcome 
of the construction, not the process. 
==PAGE:19==
State of the art 
[9] 
 
 CURRENT INITIATIVES 
Despite previous efforts, there is no indication that the renovation process has 
improved. Many big actors, associations, and foundations consequently believe there is 
still an unexploited potential for improvement in the sector. Two research projects have 
recently been launched (REBUS (Innovationsfonden 2016) and ReVALUE (Aarhus 
Universitet 2014)) based on this notion. They strive to develop new, concrete tools for 
improving both the renovation process and the results, with special focus on social housing 
projects. REBUS has quantifiable goals of reducing resource consumption in the renovation 
process by 30% and increasing productivity by 20%, and also endeavors a 50% reduction 
in energy consumption when the renovation is completed.  
An external aim of this thesis is that the research and results can be integrated in the 
two research projects REBUS and ReVALUE. 
 
==PAGE:20==
Research questions 
[10] 
 
3 RESEARCH QUESTIONS 
Previous research has shown that sustainable renovations play a crucial role in 
reaching climate goals. Due to the vast quantity of renovation projects to come it is worth 
investigating whether there is a potential for improvement in the renovation process, and 
what this improvement could be. 
Based on the generalization factor, the subject of the thesis is chosen to be social 
housing projects. 
To gain new knowledge that can be used to identify possible efficiency enhancements 
in the renovation process of social housing projects, this thesis seeks an approach that is 
different from the classic “time, quality, money”-criteria of success of a project. The focus 
is shifted towards softer, less quantitative concepts. The objectives of this thesis are based 
on the wish to maximize value creation for all stakeholders in future, similar renovation 
projects in the most resource -, time -, and energy efficient way possible. 
The following are the questions, this research seeks to answer: 
 
Who are the primary stakeholders in sustainable renovation projects of social 
housing? 
o How are their internal dynamics? 
 
What matters to the primary stakeholders in the renovation process? 
o Which aspects do stakeholders gain from? 
o Which aspects stand in the way of value creation, i.e. which aspects do 
stakeholders see as pains? 
 
Do agreements on important topics exist between stakeholders? 
o If yes, how are the interests in topics interrelated among stakeholders? 
It is beyond the scope of this report to implement – and subsequently analyze the 
results of – changes in ongoing renovation projects. However, interpretations of current 
practice as well as recommendations for future improvements are included. 
As previously stated, it is an external aim of this thesis that the research and results 
can be integrated in the two research projects REBUS and ReVALUE. 
 
 
==PAGE:21==
Methodology 
[11] 
 
4 METHODOLOGY 
In this chapter, the methodologies applied in the research are described in detail. 
Case study research is used as a framework for collecting empirical data. Data is collected 
from one main case and two secondary, comparative cases. Both qualitative and, 
subsequently, quantitative methods are used to collect data. The chapter also comprises 
explanations of the trustworthiness of the research, and an explanation of the choice of 
cases. Finally, there is a short description of the approaches used for searching literature. 
4.1 CASE STUDY RESEARCH 
The contemporary nature of the presented research questions makes a case study a 
suitable method of research to seek answers.  
Some believe that case study research is only preliminary and cannot be used to test 
propositions and build theory. Yet, this view has been challenged by several social 
scientists (Eisenhardt 1989; Flyvbjerg 2006; Yin 2014), as is discussed later in the report. 
Case study research is applicable in many situations, including studying 
organizational and managerial behavior (Yin 2014). A case study gives the researcher an 
opportunity to get a holistic, real-world perspective on a complex social phenomena, which 
is what is needed in order to answer the current research questions. As described earlier, 
building renovations are highly complex, and they are social in the sense that extensive 
communication is needed among the many involved stakeholders. 
The research questions are sought answered through one representative case study, 
and the findings are subsequently validated by comparison with two other cases.  
Afterwards, a survey template is developed and a survey conducted to quantify the results 
of the case study. 
This case study research is based on the principles presented by Robert K. Yin in his 
book “Case Study Research – Design and Methods” (Yin 2014). Yin defines five important 
components of the research design in case study research:  
1. 
Research questions 
2. 
Propositions 
3. 
Unit(s) of analysis 
4. 
The logic linking the data to the propositions 
5. 
The criteria for interpreting the findings 
The research questions are stated earlier in this report. Some propositions for the 
research questions are:  
 
Important aspects to stakeholders in relation to the renovation process exist 
beyond the classical “time, quality, money”-frame, and; 
 
Some stakeholders agree on which aspects are important, but not all. 
Consequently, conflicts and alliances of interests are created. 
==PAGE:22==
Methodology 
[12] 
 
The third component, the unit of analysis, has to do with the approach to the 
research. In this case study, the selected renovation project is the unit of analysis. The 
sources of information are chosen based on their relation to the project (both interview 
respondents and documents). The focus is on the process of the renovation, and how 
different stakeholders perceive this process. 
The fourth component, the logic linking of the data to the propositions, has to do 
with the analytical strategy chosen for the research, to be able to produce empirically based 
findings. In this case study, data is analyzed in several different ways. Data derived from 
project documents from the main case is examined to extract facts about the case and the 
process so far. The theory of Social Network Analysis (see chapter 5.3) is used to map the 
communication links between respondents. Data from qualitative interviews with key 
stakeholders is examined and categorized in a “pains and gains dictionary“, based on 
terms from the theory of the Value Proposition Canvas (see chapter 5.2). The “pains and 
gains dictionary” is a table, where the main value creating and - inhibiting aspects in the 
renovation process mentioned in the interviews are listed for each respondent. The table is 
made with inspiration from (Munk 2014), who made a similar “issue dictionary” when 
mapping “Wind Energy Controversies Online”. The table shows if there are any common 
gains and pains among the respondents. If so, these “alliances” of common gains and pains 
are shown in further tables and Social Network Maps. 
The fifth component, the criteria for interpreting the findings, is easier to address in 
cases that rely on the use of statistics, which this case study does not. In this case, the criteria 
can be fulfilled by identifying and addressing rival explanations for the findings. When 
rivals are addressed and rejected, the credibility of the findings is enhanced. 
4.2 DATA COLLECTION 
Data is collected qualitatively mainly through semi-structured interviews with 
primary stakeholders from an ongoing project, and quantitatively through two surveys; 
one among residents of multiple social housing departments, which are undergoing a 
renovation, and another among members of project teams, who are currently carrying out 
a renovation. As stated above, the quantitative survey is developed to validate the results 
of the qualitative analysis. 
The qualitative data is collected through on-site-observations from an ongoing 
sustainable building renovation of a post-war built social housing department in the 
Aarhus area. The data consists of interviews with key stakeholders, direct non-participant-
observations on site and at meetings, and project documents such as brochures of 
information aimed at the residents. The data is used to map the important aspects for each 
stakeholder, and to create an information flow network. Key positive and negative topics 
from the data are compiled in a table to form the “pains and gains dictionary”. 
The secondary data source, the surveys for collecting quantitative data, consists of 
questions regarding both residents’ and members of project teams’ opinions on the topics 
==PAGE:23==
Methodology 
[13] 
 
in the pains and gains dictionary. The majority of survey questions are introduced with 
“How important is it to you that…”, and answers are given on a five-level Likert scale. In 
this way, the degree of broader legitimacy of the qualitative data is determined. 
  QUALITATIVE INTERVIEWS 
The qualitative interviews conducted for this thesis are semi-structured “life world” 
interviews, which seek to obtain descriptions of the respondent’s life world in order to 
interpret the meaning of the described phenomena (Kvale & Brinkmann 2015 p. 177). The 
interviews have an order of subjects, they wish to cover, but consist of mostly open-ended 
questions, to have the opportunity to follow up answers with new questions, and be able 
to make slight changes in the direction of the interview. A complete interview guide can 
be found in appendix A2. The line of questioning differs depending on the respondent’s 
role in the project, and some questions are left out in some interviews, where they are not 
found to be relevant.  
Most of the prepared interview questions are based on the Value Proposition Canvas 
(see chapter 5.2). As explained in chapter The Value Proposition Canvas5.2, the questions 
in the canvas facilitate revealing pains and gains of the “customer”, which in this case are 
the stakeholders in the renovation project.  
All interviews are conducted as personal interviews. Face-to-face interviews tend to 
result in longer and more honest answers than e.g. written correspondence or phone 
interviews (Marshall 2016). Another benefit is that it is possible to see the respondent’s 
immediate reaction to a question. Respondents are asked if they want to participate in an 
interview. This way of choosing respondents can lead to some biased (e.g. only positive) 
answers. But, on the other hand, as long as the respondents are informed that they can 
speak freely, this approach ensures the most honest and elaborating answers, according to 
Shenton (Shenton 2004).  
The “snowball technique”, as proposed by Rowley (Rowley 1997), is used to 
determine which respondents to ask to participate. Initially, a key stakeholder in the project 
is interviewed. This stakeholder is chosen based on the stakeholder analysis. The first 
respondent mentions other key stakeholders, who are then interviewed. Through 
interviews with these stakeholders, other stakeholders are mentioned, etc. This process is 
iterated, ideally until the system is saturated, that is, until respondents begin to repeat each 
other. Due to the timeframe of the current research, unfortunately, the iterations had to be 
stopped before complete saturation was reached. 
During the interviews, the prepared questions are asked as a natural part of the 
conversation to the greatest extent possible, so that the respondent does not feel 
interrogated. For the same reason “how” questions are preferred as opposed to “why” 
questions, as suggested by Yin (Yin 2014).  
==PAGE:24==
Methodology 
[14] 
 
Interviews are conducted in the respondents’ native language, which is Danish for 
all respondents, and all interviews have been recorded and subsequently thoroughly 
summarized (summaries can be found in appendix A4). To include quotations in this 
report, parts of the interviews are translated.  
A total of 18 interviews with in total 20 respondents have been conducted (see 
appendix A3 for a list of respondents). They all lasted between 30 minutes and two hours 
and took place in October, November, and December 2016. The interviews have been 
conducted in two rounds. In the first round, 15 interviews have been conducted with 
respondents from the main case. The second round consisted of three expert interviews 
with three representatives from two other social housing organizations currently dealing 
with renovations. Their experience within the fields of social housing and renovations is 
what justifies referring to them as experts. The 15 primary interviews are distributed 
among stakeholders as follows;  
 
seven with residents, who are in different phases of the renovation;  
 
five with representatives of the housing association, four of whom work in the 
project office on site, and;  
 
three with a total of four representatives of the turnkey contractor, whereof three 
are working on site. 
The primary focus of the data collection is to acquire information about gains and 
pains for each stakeholder. The questions have been slightly modified for the second round 
of interviews. To avoid bias in the answers, the experts in the second round of interviews 
have not been asked to directly confirm or deny statements from the first round of 
interviews. Rather, they have been asked almost the same questions, and the similarities 
and dissimilarities in the answers will make for the confirmation or denial of findings. 
From the collected data, value creating and value inhibiting aspects mentioned by 
the different stakeholders are identified, categorized and presented in the pains and gains 
dictionary. 
 SURVEY 
As stated above, a survey is carried out to explore whether the gains and pains 
revealed through the qualitative interviews are accurate in a broader context, and to 
quantify the importance of the gains and pains. Two survey templates are developed, one 
for residents in social housing departments who are or have recently been going through 
a sustainable renovation, and one for members of project teams (social housing 
organizations, contractors, consultants etc.) who are or recently have been involved in a 
sustainable renovation of a social housing department. The templates can be seen in 
appendix A5. They consist of three parts; demographic questions, questions regarding the 
renovation process, and questions regarding the result of the renovation. The vast majority 
of questions are based on the gains and pains established through the qualitative data 
==PAGE:25==
Methodology 
[15] 
 
collection process. A few additional questions regarding e.g. sustainability and rent 
increase are added in the survey. 
The survey among residents have been carried out in two rounds. In the first round, 
it has been handed out personally to all respondents, which solely consisted of residents 
in the main project. The eight responses in this round revealed a need for honing the 
questions in the survey, since several were misunderstood by the respondents. The 
improved survey template has been created online and distributed among both residents 
from the main case (excluding the respondents from the first round of answers), and 
residents from one of the two comparative cases, giving a total possible amount of 
respondents of around 430 people. Unfortunately, only 12 answers have been collected, 
which is not enough to use as base for generalization of the answers. This deficiency is 
discussed later. 
The survey among members of project teams has been distributed online to 10 of the 
12 project team respondents from the qualitative interviews. Seven answers have been 
collected. This feedback is also not enough to conclude from, which is also included in the 
discussion. 
 TRUSTWORTHINESS OF RESEARCH 
To make the findings of this case study research as trustworthy as possible, the four 
criteria proposed by Egon G. Guba and explicated by Andrew K. Shenton are incorporated 
to the furthest extent possible (Shenton 2004). Criteria together with a brief elaboration are 
shown in Table 4-1 below. 
Qualitative  
analysis concept 
Counterpart in 
quantitative analysis 
Elaboration 
Credibility 
Internal validity 
Ensuring that the case study actually 
measures what is intended. 
Transferability 
External validity 
The extent to which the ﬁndings of one 
study can be applied to other situations. 
Dependability 
Reliability 
The degree to which if the work were 
repeated, in the same context, with the 
same methods and with the same 
participants, similar results would be 
obtained. 
Confirmability 
Objectivity 
Ensuring that the work’s ﬁndings are the 
result of the experiences and ideas of the 
informants, not the researcher. 
Table 4-1 The four criteria for trustworthiness (inspiration from (Shenton 2004)) 
Credibility: One way to establish credibility is triangulation, which is also described 
by Eisenhardt (Eisenhardt 1989). It can be both 1) triangulation of research methods, as 
used in this study: Data is collected mainly through individual interviews, but observation 
of different on-site meetings and various project documents also contribute, and findings 
==PAGE:26==
Methodology 
[16] 
 
are quantified by conducting a survey, and; 2) triangulation of data sources.  The 
triangulation of data sources is a key method in this study. The same topic (dwelling 
renovation) is studied from the view of a diversity of informants, and their statements are 
verified against each other to check for similarities or dissimilarities. This is done to 
discover the entire range of gains and pains that exist for every individual stakeholder, to 
be able to address them in the future.  
Transferability: Transferability of a qualitative case study is said to be an 
impossibility by some (Shenton 2004). Yet Robert K. Yin argues that transferability (or 
generalization) is possible if being handled the right way. He explains two ways of 
generalization; statistical and analytic. Statistical generalization is commonly used to make 
deductions about e.g. a population based on a sample of empirical data, i.e. to extrapolate 
probabilities. As an example, Yin uses an exit poll at an election, where findings among a 
sample of respondents are applied to a larger population. In this context, researchers 
determine the confidence of the extrapolation by following quantitative procedures. 
Statistical generalization is not suitable for case studies, but analytical generalization is. 
According to Yin, the case should not be thought of as a sample, but rather as an “[…] 
opportunity to shed empirical light about some theoretical concepts or principles […]” (Yin 2014, 
p. 40), which is what analytical generalization is about. In other words, it is about 
expanding and generalizing theories; striving for generalizable findings and lessons 
learned that go beyond the specific settings of the case studied. This is also what this 
research strives for. By presenting pains and gains among the interviewed stakeholders in 
the social housing renovation process, and by verifying them through stakeholders in other 
social housing organizations, which are also carrying out renovation projects, the aim is 
that the findings can be used to optimize future, similar renovations. 
Dependability: Ensuring that, if the same case study was repeated, findings and 
conclusions would be the same, is hard in qualitative research because of the changing 
nature of the phenomena investigated, according to Shenton (Shenton 2004). Observations 
are tied to the situation of the study, and shows a static picture. One way of approaching 
the dependability problem is by documenting the procedures followed in the case study. 
Shenton presents three sections that should be included for the reader to develop a 
thorough understanding of the study processes and effectiveness; 1) the research design, 
2) the operational detail of data gathering, and 3) reflective appraisal of the project 
(Shenton 2004, p. 71-72). In this thesis, the explicit research design and the thorough 
description of the data gathering process above comprise the documentation of the 
procedure, and the discussion later in the report contains an evaluation on the effectiveness 
of the process and the chosen methods. 
Confirmability: Like dependability, the criteria of confirmability is challenging to 
fulfill in qualitative research. Possible investigator bias can be reduced by the 
aforementioned triangulation and detailed description of methodology, which allows the 
reader to assess the integrity of the findings. An admission of the researcher’s prior beliefs 
==PAGE:27==
Methodology 
[17] 
 
and assumptions together with a recognition of shortcomings in the methods used also 
contribute to strengthening the confirmability of the study. All of these measures are 
included in the present research. 
 CASE SELECTION 
Choosing the right case to study for this project is crucial for its success. Numerous 
renovation projects in all scales and levels are carried out these years in all of Denmark, 
but not all of these projects are relevant to this study. The chosen case must be of 
generalizable character for the research results to fulfill the criterion of transferability and 
thus be applicable to future, similar renovation projects. For this reason, some inclusion- 
and exclusion criteria for the possible cases have been formulated in order to make a 
judicious choice. The criteria are shown in Table 4-2 below. 
Inclusion criteria 
Exclusion criteria 






to follow the project closely



part of a “general plan”

and consisting of several 
individual projects*)  


research projects REBUS 
and ReVALUE (to make the 
results equally valid for 
both projects) 
*General plans are often developed due to social problems in the area, and since 
they are not the focus of this research, both the scale and the content of general plan 
projects make them unsuitable for this study. 
Table 4-2 Selection criteria for the case study case 
Using these criteria, a thorough search online reveal four suitable renovation 
projects: 
1) 
Boligkontoret Århus: Dept. 23 – Vorregårds Allé, Nydamsvej, Illerupvej. Built 
in 1958. Comprehensive renovation in 2015-2017. 
2) 
ALBOA: Dept. 28 – Gyvelparken. Built in 1967-68. Renovation of bathrooms 
taking place from August 2016 to May 2017. 
3) 
Århus Omegn: Dept. 2 – Rosenhøj. Built in 1967-70. Comprehensive energy 
renovation taking place in 2014-2017. 
4) 
Al2bolig: Dept. 110 and 310 – Åbyhøjgård. Built in 1967-68. Energy renovation 
of façades and internal renewal of apartments is being carried out in 2015-2018. 
When reaching out to the four housing associations, it became clear that the 
renovation of Rosenhøj is part of a general plan called “Helhedsplan for Viby Syd”, and 
thus it has been excluded as main case. However, the technical director and the director of 
information of the social housing organization in charge of the project, Århus Omegn, have 
==PAGE:28==
Methodology 
[18] 
 
been used as expert respondents, and have been interviewed to confirm or deny findings 
from the main case from their perspective. 
An introductory interview with Boligkontoret Århus revealed many interesting 
topics regarding their ongoing renovation, but, unfortunately, the association did not have 
the resources to contribute to this research. 
Both ALBOA and Al2bolig have been very positive towards taking part in the 
research. Since Al2bolig’s renovation of Åbyhøjgård is the most comprehensive of the two, 
and includes more elements of sustainability than the renovation of Gyvelparken for 
ALBOA, Åbyhøjgård has been chosen as the main case for the research. The project 
manager on the Gyvelparken project has been interviewed to confirm or deny findings 
from Åbyhøjgård. 
4.3 LITERATURE SEARCH 
For the most part, the literature used in this thesis has been found online in the form 
of articles and conference proceedings. The main databases searched are: Google Scholar, 
DTU FindIt, Sciencedirect and tandfonline.com. References from articles initially found 
have been used to discover further relevant reading. 
A small sample of the keywords used for searching publications: Stakeholder 
networking, renovation management, stakeholder management in construction, user 
involvement in construction, sustainable renovation, social network analysis in 
construction, etc. Also, recurring authors such as Stephen D. Pryke, Lauri J. Koskela, C. J. 
Katsanis and Charles Egbu have been used as keywords for the literature search. 
Some 30 journal articles and conference proceedings have been carefully read as well 
as a number of books and parts of books. The books include two reports on renovation of 
public housing departments published by the Danish Building Research Institute (abbrev. 
SBi), “Case Study Research” by Robert K. Yin and the handbook of social housing 
“Håndbog om almene boliger”. “Håndbog om almene boliger” (Houlind & Edlund 2016) 
contains all the important basic knowledge concerning social housing; relevant laws, 
regulations and procedures. 
 
==PAGE:29==
Theory 
[19] 
 
5 THEORY 
Three theories are used in combination in this research; stakeholder theory, Social 
Network Analysis, and the Value Proposition Canvas. The stakeholder theory is used to 
answer the first research question and determine which stakeholders to interview for the 
qualitative data collection. The Value Proposition Canvas is used to facilitate the empirical 
data collection, that is, to design the interview questions. In this way, it is used to answer 
the second and the third research question. Social Network Analysis is used to process the 
collected data and answer the sub-question of the first research question, as well as the 
third research question. 
5.1 STAKEHOLDER THEORY 
In 1984, R. Edward Freeman published ”Stakeholder management: A Strategic 
Approach” (Freeman 1984), which has since become highly acknowledged as the 
foundation for stakeholder theory as it is known and used within many business areas 
today. According to Freeman, stakeholder theory is “an idea about how business really works. 
For any business to be successful, it has to create value for customers, suppliers, employees, 
communities, financiers – shareholders, banks, and other people who hold the money.” 
(Corporateethics 2009). He also states that any one of the stakeholders cannot be looked at 
in isolation, and that each group is important. Their interests have to go together, and the 
job of a business manager is to figure out how the interests of stakeholders go in the same 
direction (Corporateethics 2009).  
Stakeholder theory is widely used within project management, where many different 
definitions of stakeholders have been established by different researchers through the 
years. In the American National Standard, “A Guide to the Project Management Body of 
Knowledge (PMBOK Guide)”, stakeholders are defined as persons or organizations “who 
are actively involved in the project or whose interests may be positively or negatively affected by the 
performance or completion of the project. Stakeholders may also exert inﬂuence over the project, its 
deliverables, and the project team members.” (PMI 2008, p. 23).  
 STAKEHOLDER ANALYSIS 
Stakeholder analysis is a method within stakeholder theory used to map out the 
parties in a project together with the importance of each party. The stakeholders in 
construction projects in specific vary a lot. According to Calvert, they can include, but are 
not limited to “...owners and users of facilities, project managers, facilities managers, designers, 
shareholders, legal authorities, employees, subcontractors, suppliers, process and service providers, 
competitors, banks, insurance companies, media, community representative, neighbours, general 
public, government establishments, visitors, customers, civil institutions, etc.”(Calvert 1995, p. 
3). 
The PMBOK Guide states that both internal (primary) and external (secondary) 
stakeholders must be identified in order to determine the expectations and requirements 
of all parties. The primary stakeholders are the most important, because they have direct 
==PAGE:30==
Theory 
[20] 
 
stakes in the project. The stakes of the secondary stakeholders are indirect. However, the 
secondary stakeholders can be very important as well, for instance in affecting the 
reputation and public attitude towards the project. The level of responsibility and 
authority, stakeholders have on a project, vary and can change during the project life cycle, 
which means secondary stakeholders can change group and become primary, and vice 
versa. The influence of the various stakeholders in relation to the project requirements 
must be managed throughout the process of the project to ensure a successful outcome 
(PMI 2008). 
The primary ambition of this research is not to map the stakeholders, rather to look 
at their internal dynamics in regards to information flow and potential alliances of 
interests. Yet, as an initial approach to determine which stakeholders to focus on when 
collecting empirical data, a brief analysis is carried out of the stakeholders in the main case.  
5.2 THE VALUE PROPOSITION CANVAS 
The original view shown above of ‘value creation’ in stakeholder management, 
stated by R. Edward Freeman in (Corporateethics 2009), is a central point in this thesis. 
Investigating the value-adding aspects (or gains) as well as the aspects, which inhibit value 
creation according to the primary stakeholders in sustainable renovations of social housing 
projects, is the key to reveal possible areas of improvement. The Value Proposition Canvas, 
known from product development in the retail industry, is believed to be suitable for this 
purpose. A simple form of the canvas can be seen in Figure 5-1. 
 
Figure 5-1 The Value Proposition Canvas (Strategyzer 2014) 
When a retail company develops new products, the customers’ needs and wishes are 
central to the development process, just as the needs and wishes of the stakeholders in 
construction projects. The problems and issues, the customers experience, e.g. with current 
products, are also important. According to Aapaoja et al., “it is essential to take stakeholders 
and their opinions into account in the construction industry, just like in product development” 
(Aapaoja et al. 2013, p. 3). In construction projects where user involvement is applied, this 
stakeholder and their opinions are usually taken into account thereby, as earlier stated. 
==PAGE:31==
Theory 
[21] 
 
The Value Proposition Canvas, shown in Figure 5-1, Figure 5-2, and Figure 5-3, 
provides questions that help product developers in the retail industry to identify “gains” 
and “pains” among customers. This puts the product developer in a better position to 
develop useful value propositions and create a successful product. In this research, instead 
of a “product”, the approach of the VPC is applied to sustainable renovation projects of 
social housing dwellings, more specifically, the process of the renovations. The 
“customers” are the primary stakeholders in the process, in this research the interview 
respondents. Questions from the canvas, shown in Figure 5-2 and enlarged in Figure 5-3, 
are used as interview questions, as stated in chapter 4.2.1. The questions are slightly 
modified, but only to make them make sense in the construction process context. The 
structure of the canvas is explained in detail in the following sections. 
 
Figure 5-2 The Value Proposition Canvas with questions (Strategyzer 2014) 
 STRUCTURE OF THE VALUE PROPOSITION CANVAS 
The VPC is shown in Figure 5-2 above. It consists of two parts; the customer segment, 
which the business wants to create value for (right side circle), and the value propositions, 
which should attract customers (left side square) (Strategyzer 2014). In other words, the 
VPC shows the fit between what customers want, and what the business offers. In the retail 
industry, the VPC can be used as a plug-in to business models. 
The right side of the canvas shows the customer profile. It consists of three parts that 
describe the characteristics of the customer; jobs, pains, and gains. Jobs can be the tasks the 
customers are carrying out, problems they are trying to solve, or a need they are trying to 
satisfy. Jobs can have a functional, social or emotional intent, and they can be more or less 
essential. The pains are, as also explained in the glossary, the related negative aspects that 
the customers would like to avoid when carrying out jobs. Pains could be negative 
emotions, costs or risks, and they can have different degrees of importance to the customer. 
Some questions that can help elucidate customer pains are; what are the main challenges 
==PAGE:32==
Theory 
[22] 
 
the customer encounter, and; what risks does the customer fear. All the questions are 
shown in Figure 5-3. The gains, as also explained in the glossary, describe the positive 
outcomes and benefits the customers either require, expect, desire, or would be surprised 
by. Gains include positive emotions, cost savings, functional utility, and social gains, in 
other words, all value-adding aspects. Examples of questions that can clarify gains for the 
customer are; what outcome does the customer expect; what would make the customer’s 
life or job easier, and; how does the costumer measure success or failure. All the questions 
related to gains are also shown in Figure 5-3. Like with the pains, the degree of importance 
of gains to each customer varies (Strategyzer 2014). 
 
Figure 5-3 Enlarged: The part of the VPC used in this research 
The left side of the canvas shows the value proposition map (see Figure 5-1 and 
Figure 5-2). The features of the value propositions should be designed to address the 
GAINS  
Which saving would make your customer happy? 
What outcomes does your customer expect and what would go 
beyond his/her expectations? 
               How do current solutions delight your customer? 
                     What would make your customer’s job or life easier? 
               What positive social consequences does your customer desire?  
          How does your customer measure success and failure? 
      What would increase the likelihood of adopting a solution? 
   What are customers looking for? 
 What do customers dream about? 
 
What does your customer find too costly? 
  What makes your customer feel bad? 
      How are current solutions underperforming for your customer? 
          What are the main difficulties and challenges your customer encounters? 
               What negative social consequences does your customer encounter or fear? 
                    What risks does your customer fear? 
             What’s keeping your customer awake at night? 
    What common mistakes does your customer make? 
                What barriers are keeping your customer from adopting solutions? 
PAINS  
==PAGE:33==
Theory 
[23] 
 
customers’ most important jobs, pains and gains. The value proposition map is composed 
by three parts that corresponds to the customer profile; products and services, pain 
relievers, and gain creators. The products and services part includes the products and 
services, which are offered to customers to help them get a functional, social or emotional 
job done, and to address their pains and gains. Pain relievers outline explicitly how the 
products and services ease specific customer pains in relation to doing a job. The gain 
creators describe the positive outcomes and benefits, the products and services create for 
the customer (Strategyzer 2014).  
In the context of this research, the focus is on the right side of the canvas, since the 
aim is to reveal gains and pains for the stakeholders. These can then be used as inspiration 
to develop the left side of the canvas, the value propositions, for future renovation projects. 
5.3 SOCIAL NETWORK ANALYSIS 
Since R. Freeman published his revolutionary book on stakeholder theory, numerous 
research perspectives on the topic have been developed (Yang et al. 2011). One of the 
perspectives, which is used in this research, is presented by, among others, Timothy J. 
Rowley. It is the idea that stakeholders are not only connected to a firm or project, but also 
to each other (Rowley 1997), thus creating a stakeholder network. This principle generates 
the possibility to combine the conducted stakeholder analysis with a social science theory, 
Social Network Analysis. 
Social Network Analysis (abbrev. SNA) is a structural approach within social science, 
based on the study of interaction among social actors (Freeman 2004). It includes 
measuring and mapping relationships and flows between people, groups, organizations 
etc., and provides both a mathematical and visual analysis (Krebs 2013). The literature on 
SNA as a methodology within social science research is extensive. According to Malisiovas 
and Song and Chinowsky et al. (Malisiovas & Song 2014; Chinowsky et al. 2008), the 
application of SNA to explore the existence and strength of connections among groups as 
well as individual actors within organizations has been widely used ever since its 
introduction in 1934.  
 SOCIAL NETWORK MAPPING 
Social Network Mapping is a tool within SNA. Social Network Maps rely heavily on 
sociometric data (Tichy et al. 1979). They are illustrated by first ascertaining relationships 
between units, and then mapping the degree to which one unit is connected to all other 
units. Relationships and direction of flow of information between the involved groups and 
individuals are shown as patterns in the network maps with lines and nodes, respectively 
(Stanley & Katherine 1994). Various software, which can create Social Network Maps have 
been developed. A simple example of a network map, made with the software Gephi, is 
shown in Figure 5-4. 
==PAGE:34==
Theory 
[24] 
 
 
Figure 5-4 Example of a Social Network Map (made with the software Gephi) 
According to (Tichy et al. 1979), three kinds of properties are of interest in social 
networks; transactional content, nature of the links, and structural characteristics. 
Transactional content has to do with what two actors exchange; affect, information, goods 
etc. The nature of the links is shown in the strength (intensity) and reciprocity. In Figure 5-4 
above, both directed (arrow) and reciprocal (no arrow) links are present. The structural 
characteristics involve calculable concepts like clustering, density, and centrality. In the 
current research, the density concept is one of the concepts of interest.  Density is a measure 
of how many connections exist out of the total possible amount. If all possible connections 
exist, the network is said to be “fully integrated”. 
 USE OF SNA IN CONSTRUCTION 
Several researchers have previously pointed out a beneficial connection between 
Social Network Analysis and the construction industry. 
Traditionally, project management focuses on preplanning tasks and developing 
schedules that are as detailed as possible (Chinowsky et al. 2008). This is done to identify 
issues and necessary information exchange before the project starts, in order to improve 
efficiency within the classic parameters of time, cost and quality. In his 2008 article, 
Chinowsky concludes that ”The historic approach of emphasizing a continuing reﬁnement of 
tasks as a basis for achieving high performance teams is not a viable approach to achieving signiﬁcant 
performance improvement in construction projects. Rather, it is time to recognize the key role of 
individuals within project networks, including the communication and trust that is the basis for 
achieving high performance results.”  (Chinowsky et al. 2008, p. 811). Using social network 
science is one way to identify these key individuals, and clarify the scope of the network, 
too. 
Powell concluded already in 1990 that network forms have an advantage in 
industries where innovation and customized products are demanded (Pekericli et al. 2004), 
such as construction. In a study from 2011, Yang et al. confirmed the usefulness of applying 
SNA to stakeholder management in construction (Yang et al. 2011). 
==PAGE:35==
Theory 
[25]
