Table 5-1 How the research questions are answered 
The three described theories have different areas of expertise, and are brought 
together to answer the research questions. The primary stakeholders in the main case are 
identified through a stakeholder analysis. Their internal dynamics are investigated by 
applying SNA on the collected empirical data and creating a Social Network Map. The map 
is based on communication links and information flow, and it reveals potential “missing 
links” in the communication. 
Facilitated by the VPC, interview answers can be brought together in pains and gains 
dictionaries, to answer the questions of which aspects matter to the primary stakeholders, 
both positive and negative. 
The interview answers together with the pains and gains dictionaries enables the 
assembling of further tables, which show the interrelations of interests. These interrelations 
are graphically shown using SNA. This way, the VPC enables SNA to also deal with the 
context of exchanged information, not only the presence or absence of links. 
 
==PAGE:36==
Analysis and findings 
[26] 
 
6 ANALYSIS AND FINDINGS 
This chapter consists of first a brief description of facts about the cases of the research. 
Then, the primary and key stakeholders are identified through a stakeholder analysis, 
followed by a Social Network Map of respondents showing their internal dynamics. 
Subsequently, the assembled pains and gains dictionaries are presented, as well as tables 
and a SNM showing interrelations and alliances of interests. Lastly, the identified pains 
and gains are presented one by one. 
6.1 CASE FACTS 
Facts about the primary case; the renovation of Åbyhøjgård, as well as about the two 
secondary cases; Gyvelparken and Rosenhøj, have been collected through interviews, 
project web sites, and documents provided by residents and other stakeholders. The 
complete description of facts can be found in appendix A1. 
 PRIMARY CASE: ÅBYHØJGÅRD 
The main case in this study is the social housing departments Åbyhøjgård, which 
comprise the two departments; no. 110 and no. 310, under the social housing organization 
Al2bolig. Åbyhøjgård consists of ten blocks of apartments of different sizes, with 1 to 4 
bedrooms. The renovation of Åbyhøjgård is extensive, and can be divided in outdoor and 
indoor works. The complete scope of the renovation is shown in Table 1 in appendix A1. 
The project has been tendered as a turnkey contract, and the bidding was won by MT 
Højgaard (abbrev. MTH). The renovation is carried out in different stages, block by block. 
Outdoor tasks are initiated first as they comprise the most comprehensive work. Then, 
work begins in the halls and stairways. There are three to four stairways in each block. 
Finally, indoor works are carried out. When the craftsmen begin working inside the 
apartments, the residents move to a temporary relief housing facility placed on site. They 
leave most of their furniture in the apartments, piled up in assigned places, and shielded 
or wrapped in plastic. The residents have access to their apartments during the entire 
renovation process, and are advised by Al2bolig to check up on their things, e.g. that the 
refrigerator is still running. The residents are moved to the temporary housing facilities 
one stairway at a time, and stay there for eight weeks before they return to the renovated 
apartment. Residents from one stairway are moved in and out of the temporary housing 
every other week, so on average, indoor work is carried out in four stairways at a time.  
The renovation began in September 2015, and the first indoor works were initiated 
in December 2015. The entire renovation project is expected to be completed in July 2018. 
 SECONDARY CASES: GYVELPARKEN AND ROSENHØJ 
 One of the two secondary cases, used as comparison and for validation of data 
gathered from Åbyhøjgård, is the social housing department Gyvelparken, which is 
administered by the social housing organization ALBOA. Gyvelparken consists of 69 
apartments with 1, 2, or 4 bedrooms, placed in two story row houses. Residents live in their 
==PAGE:37==
Analysis and findings 
[27] 
 
apartments during the renovation, but since the water is cut off, mobile kitchens and 
bathrooms are installed outside for the residents to use. Other facts have been stated in 
chapter 4.2.4.  
The comprehensive renovation of Rosenhøj is the second of the two comparative 
cases. Rosenhøj consists of 808 dwellings, and is administered by the social housing 
organization Århus Omegn. Most of the 808 dwellings are located in four story apartment 
blocks, 22 are placed in row houses, which have been erected as part of the renovation 
project.  
Rosenhøj covers a large area of ground. Thus, it has been possible for Århus Omegn 
to carry out the renovation in stages, covering different parts of the area at a time. This has 
been done to avoid a feeling among the residents of living on a construction site during the 
entire project, which lasts around three years. 
6.2 STAKEHOLDER ANALYSIS 
As stated earlier, as an initial approach to determine which stakeholders to focus on 
when collecting empirical data, a brief stakeholder analysis is carried out. The stakeholders 
of the main case, Åbyhøjgård, are first identified as primary and secondary stakeholders 
according to the description in chapter 5.1.1, see Table 6-1 below. Then, they are placed in 
a power-interest-matrix according to their power on and interest in the process of the 
renovation. The matrix is shown in Figure 6-1. 
Primary (direct) stakeholders 
Secondary (indirect) stakeholders 
Client/users/financier (residents) 
Neighbors 
Client advisor (Al2bolig) 
Government 
Architects, engineers (consultants) 
Legal authorities 
Turnkey contractor (MT Højgaard) 
Employees 
Sub-contractors 
Suppliers 
Financier (the DNBF) 
Media 
Table 6-1 Primary and secondary stakeholders in the Åbyhøjgård project 
Table 6-1 shows six primary stakeholders and six secondary stakeholders. The 
primary stakeholders are the ones of interest to this research. Of the six primary 
stakeholders, four are placed in the “Key player” quadrant of the power-interest-matrix in 
Figure 6-1. 
==PAGE:38==
Analysis and findings 
[28] 
 
 
Figure 6-1 Power-interest matrix of Åbyhøjgård stakeholders 
The reason that sub-contractors are not in this quadrant, is the form of contract on 
the Åbyhøjgård project, which means the turn-key contractor, MT Højgaard, is ultimately 
responsible for the sub-contractors. This is also the reason that the consultants are placed 
slightly lower on both scales. The DNBF is a primary stakeholder, since it partly funds the 
renovation project (explained in the glossary and in appendix A1 Case facts), but it is not 
involved in the process of construction, which is why it is placed in the “Keep satisfied” 
quadrant.  
The board of departments is not mentioned as an independent stakeholder in Table 
6-1, since it consists of residents, as explained in the glossary. However, as the board of 
department has the authorization to make decisions on behalf of the rest of the residents, 
they have more power in the process of the renovation, and for this reason they are given 
an independent location in the matrix. 
 The location of stakeholders in the power-interest matrix provide a basis for 
choosing interview respondents. Since the housing organization, Al2bolig, is the face of the 
project, they are the best choice of approach to the project. This also justifies the choice to 
interview representatives from the housing organizations managing the two secondary 
cases, Rosenhøj and Gyvelparken. The other respondents from Åbyhøjgård represent the 
residents (including the board), and MT Højgaard. Unfortunately, do to time constraints, 
consultants have not been interviewed.  
==PAGE:39==
Analysis and findings 
[29] 
 
6.3 SOCIAL NETWORK MAP 
Through the conducted interviews, information on communication links between 
stakeholders in the Åbyhøjgård renovation project has been collected. The information 
comes from both direct interview questions such as “Which stakeholders are you in contact 
with?”, and “Who is the most important stakeholder to you?” and from indications in 
answers to other questions.  
The information is used to construct a Social Network Map of the flow of 
communication in the project, thus answering the research question regarding the internal 
dynamics between stakeholders. The map is seen in Figure 6-2 below. It is limited to cover 
interview respondents. Since there is no significant deviation in the connections mentioned 
by the seven residents interviewed, they are shown as a single node. Though some of the 
communication links may not be completely reciprocal, none of the links represent entirely 
one-way communication. Consequently, the lines do not have arrows. 
 
Figure 6-2 Social Network Map of respondents from the main case (made with the software Gephi) 
The nodes in the map are proportioned in size according to the degree to which they 
are connected with the other nodes (i.e. the number of connections they have). The color of 
each node indicates which stakeholder the respondent represents. 
From the interview answers it can be deduced that no necessary links are missing in 
the network of communication in the Åbyhøjgård project, that is, no delays or other 
problems can solely be explained by missing links in the communication. This is illustrated 
in the map, which is quite well-integrated, with every respondent being connected to at 
==PAGE:40==
Analysis and findings 
[30] 
 
least two other respondents. The social housing organization (represented by the node 
“Project manager Al2bolig” in the network) and the residents are the most central 
stakeholders in the network, both with eight out of 10 connections. This underlines the 
earlier stated high importance of users/residents in a renovation. The only respondents, 
residents are not directly connected with, are the director of Al2bolig and the Head of 
section from MT Højgaard, who are the only two respondents not working on site on a 
daily basis. Information from residents that needs to reach these two respondents, can be 
communicated through the project manager from Al2bolig or the project leader from MTH. 
The two respondents, the project manager from Al2bolig is not connected with, are the 
foreman from MTH and the resident coordinator from MTH. This is justified by the 
contractual relationships of the project.  
The second most integrated stakeholder is the project manager of the turn-key 
contractor MT Højgaard, who has seven connections. The three respondents, he is not 
connected to are the resident coordinator and the maintenance manager from Al2bolig, 
and the board of residents. This is also justified by the contractual relationships of the 
project. On the rare occasion that he should need to reach out to the three, he can do so 
through the project manager from Al2bolig. 
The least connected respondent is the Foreman for brick and concrete works from 
MTH. Again, the contractual relationships between the stakeholders can explain the 
missing connections. His connection to the residents exists because he and his team of 
craftsmen work inside the residents’ apartments. 
To sum up, the network is quite well-integrated, and the number of connections for 
each respondent can be explained by the contractual relationships, as well as the physical 
layout of the project. 
6.4 PAINS AND GAINS DICTIONARIES 
Presented in this chapter are the findings that answer the research questions of which 
aspects matter to the stakeholders – both positive and negative – as well as whether 
stakeholders agree on which topics matter. All the aspects mentioned by each respondent 
during the interviews have been categorized as a gain or a pain in the process, and 
assembled in two tables of pains and gains, one for resident respondents, and one for the 
respondents from different project teams. Following each of the two pains and gains tables, 
another table shows the coupling of pains and gains to each respondent, clarifying the 
interrelations of interests among respondents. Based on the two interrelations tables, one 
combined Social Network Map is created to illustrate potential alliances of respondents. 
It is important to note that the classic ‘hard’ values such as “staying within budget”, 
and “completing the project on time” were mentioned by the majority of project team 
members. They are also implied in several of the gains and pains listed below. The money 
and time aspects are definitely very important, but, they are also already being given 
attention in contracts. The focus of this research, as previously stated, is the “softer” 
==PAGE:41==
Analysis and findings 
[31] 
 
concepts of value creation and inhibiting. Consequently, these are the ones mainly 
addressed in the following.

primary case, the renovation of Åbyhøjgård. Five pains and three gains have been 
identified. The right side of each column shows the number of residents, who mentioned 
that specific pain or gain, out of the total number of interviewed residents. In some cases, 
what is presented as a pain in the table, has also been mentioned in a positive context by a 
single resident, and vice versa. These cases do not count in the frequency, but are addressed 
later.

6.5 SOCIAL NETWORK MAP OF INTEREST ALLIANCES 
 
Figure 6-3 Social Network Map showing alliances of interests 
The network in Figure 6-3 shows the resident respondents (greenish color nodes), the 
project team respondents (grey color nodes), and all of the gains and pains from Table 6-2 
and Table 6-4 (yellow color nodes). The information on interrelations from Table 6-3 and 
Table 6-5 is applied as lines connecting each respondent to all the topics he or she has 
mentioned. Respondent nodes have a default size, but the nodes representing gains and 
pains are proportioned according to the number of respondents, who have mentioned that 
specific topic.  
Naturally, two general clusters are created containing the residents and the project 
team respondents, respectively. They are connected through three shared topics; “Errors 
and omissions”, “Promises not being kept”, and “Improved quality of dwellings”, which 
are be elaborated in the next chapter. The density of the network is a measure of the degree 
of integration, i.e. the number of connections. Nodes with many connections attract each 
other, and vice versa. The different density of the two clusters illustrate that generally there 
is a broader agreement on topics among residents than among project team respondents, 
resulting in the resident cluster being more dense than the cluster of project members. This 
matter is addressed in the discussion.

Analysis and findings

6.6 PRESENTATION OF PAINS AND GAINS 
In this chapter, the information from the tables above is elaborated. The pains and 
gains above are treated one by one in the order they are presented in the tables, starting 
with residents’ pains, continuing with residents’ gains, project team members’ pains, and 
finally project team members’ gains. As stated, one pain and two gains are shared by 
residents and the project team members. These topics are grouped and only presented 
once.  
Following the presentation of the pains and gains from the tables, an additional 
couple of topics are described, which do not fit in the above tables as either a pain or a gain.  
 ERRORS AND OMISSIONS, RP1 AND PP1 
 
 
Residents 
Members of 
project teams 
 
 
Figure 6-4 Network of respondents who state ‘errors and omissions’ as a pain 
 
“Too many errors and omissions and a too slow correction process” is a pain stated 
by six out of seven residents, and by six out of 12 of the members of different project teams. 
It should be noted that the one resident, who has not mentioned this pain, had not yet 
returned to her renovated apartment at the time of the interview. As an example of the 
problem with errors and omissions and a too slow correction process, one of the residents, 
An, says the craftsmen walked in and out of his apartment to fix errors for six months after 
it was supposed to be completed. He expected some problems in the process, since he lives 
in one of the first apartments to be renovated, but says “I was surprised it could be that bad”. 
The project team members, who stated this as a pain, are all but one part of the 
Åbyhøjgård renovation project. PeM, the respondent from ALBOA, says “the worst things 
in a construction project are budget overruns, and if there are many errors and omissions, which 
take a long time to fix”. The issue has not been mentioned by the respondents from Rosenhøj.

Figure 6-5 Network of residents who state 'promises not being kept' as a pain 
 
“Promises (in regards to both scope of renovation and time schedule) not being kept” 
is a pain stated by four of the seven residents and by four members of project teams.  
It concerns several aspects; 
 
Apartments not being finished at the time MTH and Al2bolig agreed upon, and 
the residents were told. An example is stated by resident Mu – a mother on 
maternity leave – who says she was told her terrace would be reestablished four 
weeks after she moved back home, but the work was not initiated until ten weeks 
after. 
 
The scope of the renovation. Resident Da says that to begin with, the housing 
organization promised the residents an almost completely renovated apartment 
‘as good as new built’, but then later, they modified those words. According to 
Da, this was done to moderate the expectations. 
 
New ventilation system not living up to the expectations, which were based on 
statements from the housing organization. Resident Jo is very disappointed that 
the ventilation system takes up much of the closet space in her hallway and says 
“it was not part of the original plan, we were told they only needed to use the upper part 
of the closets. It was a dirty trick from the housing organization.” Moreover, she, Da 
and An do not feel the indoor climate in their apartments has improved to the 
degree they were told it would. 
 
MTH not taking care of residents the way they promised to do in their project 
bid, according to the facility managers at Åbyhøjgård. 
As a comment on promises to residents not being kept by project team members, Al 
expresses that “The residents will put up with almost anything, as long as they know the timeframe

 LOW PRIORITY OF RESIDENTS, RP3 
 
 
 
 
Residents 
 
 
Figure 6-6 Network of residents who state 'low priority of residents' as a pain 
 
 “A feeling of not being taken seriously by the project office/contractor, being given 
a low priority in the project” is a pain mentioned explicitly by three residents. More of the 
residents recognize this pain through other statements, such as that it is challenging to get 
the housing organization and contractor to listen to them. An says “It is a big project with 
many involved parties. You easily get the feeling that you do not matter as a resident”. As also 
implied in this statement, however, several residents also express an understanding that it 
is not possible to take into account the opinion of every single resident. 
One resident, Sa, mentions a lack of attention given to the children in the area; “They 
have nowhere to play during the renovation, and it is not safe for them to be outside at all. There 
was a woman in the beginning, who did some activities with the children, but it didn’t last long”.

Figure 6-7 Network of residents stating ‘discontinuity of information’ as a pain

temporary relief housing; “In the beginning, the communication worked well. But then, it

all the residents in the affected apartment block. Members of the Åbyhøjgård project team 
admit the problem, and they organized a meeting at the time to solve the issue. Da is

 MOVING TO RELIEF HOUSING AND BACK, RP5 
 
 
 
 
 
Residents 
Figure 6-8 Network of residents stating 'moving to relief housing and back' as a pain 
 
“Moving to relief housing and back, leaving belongings in apartments” is a pain to 
five out of the seven residents. The issue was in most cases brought up as an answer to the 
interview question “What is your biggest concern in the process?”. The concerned 
residents feel that preparing the apartment for the renovation (taking down everything 
from the walls and piling furniture in specific spots in the apartment) is a big and 
unmanageable task. Moreover, they are worried that something will happen to the things 
they leave behind in their apartments. Sa mentions both of these aspects. She says “The 
biggest challenge is that we have to pack everything, even though we are not moving out” and 
continues “I am afraid that someone will break into our apartment while we are gone. They 
[Al2bolig] should have done more to prevent that. It makes me feel quite anxious”. 
Sø adds that although the task of moving is big, he appreciates that the housing 
organization provides boxes, plastic and tape to facilitate packing and wrapping his 
belongings. 
Resident Jo do not see any challenge in moving: “I just packed five boxes to take with me 
and left the rest in the apartment, it could not have been any easier.”

of the process. New residents that move in will probably be excited”. 
The director of Al2bolig, Al, mentions the great value he sees in improving the 
quality of dwellings for the residents, but at the same time, he expresses concern that “The 
current renovation does not provide enough improved living quality for the residents. The residents 
should be able to feel what they get for their investment”.

 RESIDENTS’ ACCESS TO DWELLINGS DURING RENOVATION, PP4

Figure 6-14 Network of members of project teams who state 'residents' access to

“Residents have access to dwellings during renovation” is stated as a pain by five 
out of the 12 project respondents, representing all three cases. The issue is naturally only 
relevant in the renovations, where residents still have belongings in the apartments during 
the renovation, and thus have access. Residents having access is a pain, since it complicates 
the work the craftsmen are carrying out, says JoM, who is the foreman for brick and 
concrete workers on the Åbyhøjgård renovation. “One could wish for the next project that the 
residents do not have access while we are working”, he further states. Mo and Ti recognize this 
pain in the renovation of Rosenhøj, where it generates many complaints from residents 
whose belongings have been damaged, allegedly during the renovation.

 RESIDENTS’ SATISFACTION WITH PROCESS AND RESULT, PG8

Figure 6-21 Network of members of project teams who state 'residents’ satisfaction with

“Residents’ satisfaction with process and result” is stated as a gain by six of the 12 
respondents. To both Ga and Be, this gain is the answer to the interview question: “What 
is the main criteria for success for you in regards to the process?” Ga states that “As long as 
the residents are happy, I think it [the project] is going well”. Be’s answer is “That residents are 
satisfied with both the process and the result, even though the process can be tough at times”.  
 FURTHER FINDINGS 
A couple of topics have been mentioned by several respondents, but do not classify 
as neither a pain nor a gain. These are: 
 
Procurement form: Several respondents from the project teams (e.g. PeK and 
PeM) have brought up the form of contract as a subject that is important for the 
success of a renovation project. The suitable form depends on the specific project, 
but it seems the majority believe that a turnkey contract is not optimal for a 
renovation that requires residents’ involvement. PeM explains: “In renovation 
projects, […] the consultant should look after our [the housing organization’s] interests. 
If it is a turnkey contract, there is a consultant, but he is employed by the turnkey 
contractor, which complicates the dialogue”. Moreover, he believes a turnkey 
contract for these types of projects will make them more expensive. 
 
Improving the time schedule: In line with the previously mentioned 
‘inadequate tender preparation’, some of the respondents from the project teams 
(PeK, Al, and PeS) suggest rearranging the construction activities so more time 
is available for evaluation and optimization in the beginning of the construction 
phase. Al specifically suggests looking at “[…] the optimal flow, not for the 
craftsmen, but for the residents. I have been thinking that we may be focusing too much 
on the speed of the process, because ‘time is money’. But so is the other aspect [preparing 
the residents for the process and the result]”. PeS wishes for a more flexible time 
Residents' 
satisfaction 
with process 
and result
Ga
Al
Lo
Be
Mo
Ti
==PAGE:58==
Analysis and findings 
[48] 
 
management, and further says “The processes can be optimized, and sometimes it 
leads to a better experience for the residents to have some flexibility”. He continues “If 
we could stay within the same timeframe by using two months extra on the first 
apartment block, and then saving two days on each block afterwards, it would be great, 
but we do not always have this leeway”. 
These two topics conclude the findings of this research.  
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:59==
Discussion 
[49] 
 
7 DISCUSSION 
In this chapter, first, the findings of the thesis are discussed, including interpretations 
and recommendations for future practice. Then, the methodologies and theories used to 
identify the findings are discussed. Finally, the contributions to theory based on this 
research are addressed.  
7.1 FINDINGS 
 RIVAL EXPLANATION REJECTED WITH SOCIAL NETWORK MAP 
Despite the well-integrated Social Network Map shown in Figure 6-2, the pains and 
gains dictionaries show that the Åbyhøjgård project has not been running smoothly.  
From the map it can be concluded that it is not the absence or presence of 
communication links that causes the issues mentioned in Table 6-2 and Table 6-4. Hence, 
with reference to the fifth component of the case study research design described in chapter 
4.1; the criteria for interpreting the findings, this rival explanation is rejected.  
Judging from the tables, the source of the problems is rather the content or 
discontinuity of the information exchanged between the links in Figure 6-2. Since SNA 
cannot deal with the content of the information exchanged between actors, only whether a 
link exists or not, it is not a suitable theoretical tool to process these matters. For this 
purpose, the VPC is used. Still, the created Social Network Map fulfils the purpose of 
revealing the internal dynamics between respondents, as intended. 
 GROUPING OF GAINS AND PAINS 
The aim with conducting the interviews was to learn which pains and gains each 
individual stakeholder sees in a sustainable renovation of social housing dwellings. The 
hope was that more respondents would mention the same pains and gains, showing a 
degree of agreement between the stakeholders. The way of asking open-ended questions 
have resulted in no answers from the respondents being identical to the letter. However, 
fortunately, many answers have similar wording, and even more express comparable 
opinions, which justifies assembling Table 6-2 and Table 6-4 with the abridged statements 
of all respondents.  
Still, the formulation, grouping, and placing of the gains and pains found through 
the interviews imply a risk of bias and thus a threat to the trustworthiness of the research. 
The gains and the pains in the tables are formulated to cover as many of the statements 
given on each topic as possible. Still, this approach has led to a couple of topics not fitting 
in the tables, as they were neither mentioned in a particularly positive nor particularly 
negative context.  
Another researcher might have couched the pains and gains slightly differently, and 
might also have placed few of the responses in different boxes. Yet, since it is not the exact 
==PAGE:60==
Discussion 
[50] 
 
phrasing of the topics that is important, rather the greater meaning, this weakness is 
considered undamaging to the findings of the research. 
From the frequencies shown in Table 6-2 and Table 6-3 it can be seen that many of the 
interviewed residents mention the same pains and gains. This can be explained by them 
belonging to the same stakeholder, and thus having the same outlook on the process to a 
large degree. For this reason, it has not been difficult to summarize the responses and create 
the pain and gain dictionary for residents.  
The challenge of summarizing responses from the inquired members of project teams 
has been much greater. This is logical, since this group of respondents represent different 
stakeholders. The members of the project teams have different outlooks on the process, and 
therefore also mention different topics as value-adding and - inhibiting. In order for Table 
6-4 not to become too comprehensive and unmanageable, interpretation of the statements 
from project team respondents has been necessary. The diversity in answers is reflected in 
the frequencies shown in Table 6-4. 
 INTERRELATION OF GAINS AND PAINS AMONG STAKEHOLDERS 
Getting more respondents to mention the same gains or pains without disclosing 
other respondents and affecting the answers, has been challenging and not always 
successful. Accordingly, some viewpoints have not been validated to the degree they could 
have been, because they could not be directly asked about without risking biasing the 
respondent.  This problem has been especially relevant in the interviews with respondents 
from the two comparative cases, since validation was the entire basis of conducting these 
interviews. 
The problem with validation also means that the frequency of each gain and pain in 
Table 6-2 and Table 6-4 cannot be seen as a representation of how many agree with each 
pain and gain, only of how many mentioned each when asked the same open questions.  
To determine the degree of agreement among stakeholders, the conducted survey 
was developed based on the found pains and gains. Unfortunately, as previously 
discussed, the feedback on the survey has not been sufficient to conclude from in this 
research, only to suggest the method be used in the future. 
It would possibly have been easier to have used a survey in the first place to make 
the respondents from the secondary cases validate findings instead of interviewing them 
with open questions, hoping to get answers similar to those already received. On the other 
hand, the aim of interviewing these respondents was not only to validate findings from the 
main case, but also to make sure the findings were fully comprehensive and that nothing 
had been missed. This aspect would have been lost, had only a survey been carried out. 
Furthermore, based on the poor feedback on the actually conducted survey, relying more 
on survey answers would merely have weakened the research.  
==PAGE:61==
Discussion 
[51] 
 
 INFLUENCE OF DIFFERENT GAINS AND PAINS ON EACH OTHER 
Some of the gains and pains identified are influenced by one another. For instance, 
the pain of “promises not being kept” is considered to some extent to root in the gain 
“alignment of expectations among residents” or rather, a lack of said alignment. The 
mentioned pain is one of the most agreed on topics among the respondents. It is found that 
they do not feel they can trust the information, they are given, whether it is from the 
contractor to the housing organization, or from the contractor or housing organization to 
the residents. This is an issue that needs attention in itself. However, since the mentioned 
pain and gain are considered to have an influence on each other, and the pain is believed 
to be rooted in a lack of alignment of expectations, the first step to eliminate this pain is 
believed to be aligning the expectations to the process and the result among the residents. 
This belief stems from some of the interview answers, especially from residents, who 
explain their dissatisfaction by saying it was not what they expected, rather than not what 
they were told. A suggestion on how to solve the issues concerning alignment of 
expectations is through mock-ups, either physical or virtual, which is suggested by Fl from 
MTH. This would ensure that the residents have a complete understanding of what they 
can expect as a final result. Another way of aligning the expectations is to use the RENO-
EVALUE tool described in chapter 2.2. 
The pain of “discontinuity of information” is also assumed to be interrelated with 
the pain of “promises not being kept” and ultimately the misalignment of expectations. In 
the interview with the MTH resident coordinator, Be, she recognizes the problem in the 
Åbyhøjgård project of disruptions in the information flow, but at the same time, she points 
to the problem that the “sufficient” amount of information is subjective and different to 
each resident. A suggestion on how to solve this dilemma in future projects is to use the 
survey template for residents developed in this research. It can help decide on the level of 
information to pass on to the residents, since it includes questions about what form of 
information, residents would like, and about when in the renovation process, they would 
like to be informed. 
Generally, a first step towards eliminating the abovementioned pains, is to follow the 
guidelines for user involvement in renovation projects developed by Værdibyg, described 
in chapter 2.3.  
 GAINS AND PAINS GIVING RISE TO FURTHER DISCUSSION 
As stated in chapter 4.2.1 Qualitative interviews, the interviews conducted are semi-
structured “life world” interviews, which seek to obtain descriptions of the respondent’s 
life world in order to interpret the meaning of the described phenomena (Kvale & 
Brinkmann 2015, p. 177). Some of the “described phenomena” in the form of important 
topics to the respondents give rise to further discussion. They are addressed in this 
subchapter. 
==PAGE:62==
Discussion 
[52] 
 
7.1.5.1 
AMBIVALENCE TOWARDS TEMPORARY RELIEF HOUSING 
Residents’ views on the temporary relief housing are ambivalent. To a large degree 
it is the same people, who see the relief housing as a gain in the process, who also mention 
the pain of moving to the relief housing and back. In many cases, they even say this is the 
biggest concern they have in the renovation process. How big the challenge is, probably 
depends on the personality and psychical strength of the resident.  
This ambivalence can be explained by the fact that the relief housing is not as much 
an actual “gain” as it is a “pain reliever”. Moving to the relief housing is not something 
that creates value for the residents as such, but it relieves the pain of having to stay in the 
apartments during the renovation.  
The gain (or pain) of the temporary relief housing is connected to a pain mentioned 
among project team respondents of residents having access to dwellings during the 
renovation. While this is mentioned solely as a pain among project team respondents, Mo 
states that, according to one of the contractors at Rosenhøj, residents leaving their 
belongings in the apartments and still having access results in the craftsmen treating the 
apartments more carefully. This is a reflection worth considering in future projects. 
7.1.5.2 
NOT POSSIBLE TO COMPLETELY ENSURE GOOD MANAGEMENT 
Management skills of project managers are crucial for a renovation project to be 
successful, according to a number of respondents. By several, they are mentioned in the 
context that a client can be tempted to choose a contractor he knows from previous 
successful projects. But, if the project team and the project manager are not the same as in 
the previous project, or if other circumstances are different, success is not guaranteed. 
Because it is very hard to quantify the skills of a good manager, it is difficult to 
include them as a request in the tender material. Moreover, predicting who will be a good 
manager under certain circumstances; in a specific project with certain constraints, 
craftsmen, residents, obstacles etc., is also problematic.  
The pain could possibly be relieved through more comprehensive and focused 
education on renovation, which would provide more project managers with the suitable 
skills to manage renovation projects. As described in chapter 2.1 Managing renovation 
projects, this focus already exists, and the subject of renovation is being increasingly 
incorporated in relevant educations. 
7.1.5.3 
LACK OF EDUCATION IN MAINTAINING NEW INSTALLATIONS 
Though not mentioned explicitly as a pain by any respondent, several respondents 
from Åbyhøjgård indicate that they do not know how to maintain the new installations in 
the apartments, e.g. the new ventilation system. This could result in residents (and 
ultimately the environment) not benefitting fully from the system. Project manager Fl 
recognizes the gap in the handover of the systems, but justifies it with the scope of the 
contract. Resident coordinator Be says the residents receive a folder in their apartment with 
information on functions of all new installations. But, she realizes that this is possibly not 
==PAGE:63==
Discussion 
[53] 
 
sufficient; “One thing is to see it in writing, but we should probably review it with them. Many of 
the residents will most likely never look in the folder”.  
To be able to benefit fully from the sustainable improvements implemented through 
sustainable renovations of social housing dwellings in the future, it is recommended to 
increase the attention given to educating residents and facility managers in the use and 
maintenance of installations. This should be done through different methods of 
communication, and not only one-way communication. 
 THE BOARD OF RESIDENTS ILL-SUITED TO MAKE DECISIONS ON BEHALF OF ALL RESIDENTS 
There are several issues when it comes to the current strategy for user involvement 
in renovations of social housing dwellings. One is that the number of opinions possibly 
equals the number of residents, which makes the task of satisfying everyone 
unmanageable. For this reason, the board of the department – consisting of residents 
elected democratically by all residents in the department – have the authorization to make 
many decisions on behalf of the rest of the residents. These decisions include matters of 
different scope, e.g. choosing colors of façade materials, course of outdoor paths and 
stairways, faucets for bathrooms and kitchens, cupboard grips etc. While having the board 
make these choices may seem like a good solution compared to having to deal with 
hundreds of residents’ different opinions, it can also present an issue. Since the board 
members are not likely to have any experience in construction or building design, they do 
not have any prerequisites for making such decisions. Moreover, as they were presumably 
not elected for board based on their taste in design, it seems rather inappropriate that they 
make these decisions. The board does get support from the housing organization – which 
does not necessarily have any experience with renovation and building design either – and 
architects, but maybe the support should be more comprehensive throughout the process.  
 SUSTAINABILITY NOT MENTIONED IN ANY OF THE INTERVIEWS 
It is a noteworthy finding of this thesis that none of the respondents mentioned 
sustainability during the interviews. As with all other topics, it was not explicitly included 
in any of the interview questions, to minimize the risk of biasing the respondents in their 
answers. Still, considering the variety of answers received to the open-ended questions 
regarding which topics the respondents’ see as important in the process, this finding is 
somewhat surprising. 
All three of the involved renovation projects qualify as sustainable renovations. For 
instance, Åbyhøjgård qualifies since it involves e.g. improving the insulation of buildings, 
and installing a ventilation system with heat recovery to reduce energy use. Though some 
of the respondents mention these improvements, not one of the respondents, either 
residents or project team members, relate them to sustainability. Bearing in mind the 
concerns mentioned in the beginning of this report, and the goals of the two research 
projects ReValue and REBUS, this is rather disquieting.  
==PAGE:64==
Discussion 
[54] 
 
It can be deduced from the interviews that sustainability is not very present in the 
respondents’ minds. One reason that residents in specific do not think about sustainability 
could be their social situation. People from the lower stratum often constitute a large part 
of the residents in social housing, and with potential unemployment, other economic 
concerns, and perhaps physical or psychical challenges, these people may not have the 
surplus energy to reflect on a rather abstract term like sustainability, which does not affect 
them personally in their everyday lives. It is more difficult to explain the lack of attention 
to sustainability among the project team respondents. Maybe it is a question of priorities. 
Time, budget, and – to some – satisfied residents come first, and do not leave room for 
sustainability beyond what is a natural consequence of improving the indoor climate of 
dwellings. If this is the case, a way to change the project members’ thinking is to increase 
the level of legislation in the area of sustainability, both regarding the outcome of the 
renovation, and the renovation process.   
No matter the reasons, a bigger effort should be considered to increase awareness 
among people of the importance of sustainability, because as long as they are not aware, it 
is hard to make them want to participate in the necessary sustainable development. 
7.2 THEORIES AND METHODOLOGIES  
 USING THE VALUE PROPOSITION CANVAS TO CONCEPTUALIZE VALUE CREATION 
As seen in other theories, such as LEAN Construction, it is not far-fetched to compare 
processes in the retail industry with the construction business, and so the same is believed 
to apply for product development in retail with process and project planning in 
construction. This justifies using the Value Proposition Canvas for this research. The 
credible comparison is also due to the goal ultimately being the same in the two industries; 
satisfying the “customer”. With the focus of the current research being the process rather 
than the outcome of renovation projects, the “product” in this case is the construction 
process, and the customers are the stakeholders involved in this process.  
As stated in chapter 3 Research questions, the objectives of this thesis are based on 
the wish to maximize value creation for all stakeholders in future, sustainable renovations 
of social housing dwellings. The Value Proposition Canvas has been used as a tool to 
conceptualize the rather abstract term of value creation. It has provided an approach that 
is comprehensible to all respondents, and has proved to be very useful for developing 
interview questions that can reveal the value-adding and value inhibiting aspects of the 
renovation process. Actually, it is believed there is a potential to use even more of the 
canvas questions in the context of construction and renovation in the future. Questions, 
which can be used are, for instance; “Which savings would make the customer happy? (e.g. 
savings of time, money, effort)”, and “How do current solutions delight the customer? (e.g. 
performance, quality, features)”. 
==PAGE:65==
Discussion 
[55] 
 
 INTERVIEWS 
7.2.2.1 
WHY QUALITATIVE INTERVIEWS INSTEAD OF A SURVEY?  
The two research questions could potentially have been answered by a survey. This 
could have been done in two ways; either by asking open-ended questions, e.g. “What are 
the most important aspects to you in the renovation process?”, or by coming up with the 
possible important aspects beforehand, and then ask for confirmation or denial – or just 
degree of agreement – on each topic, much like in the conducted validation survey. 
The problem with the first-mentioned approach is that people tend to give short 
answers in surveys, which would mean that answers would probably only show a fraction 
of topics (and there would be no way to know whether this was the case). Moreover, open-
ended questions are also more open to interpretation, which would lead to more diverse 
answers. In face-to-face interviews, the interviewer can clarify the intent of the question if 
necessary. Diverse answers would make it challenging to reach conclusions based on 
survey answers alone.  
There are several problems with the second tactic. First of all, there is no guarantee 
that the range of values and issues would match the actual values and issues among the 
stakeholders. Some topics would undoubtedly be missing in the survey, and others would 
be superfluous, not representing the view of any respondent. Coming up with the values 
and issues beforehand would in this way also be putting words in the mouths of the 
respondents, and possibly affect them to have an opinion on something that had never 
crossed their mind. 
Based on these arguments, it is believed that the choice to answer the research 
questions with semi-structured, qualitative interviews was right for this research. 
7.2.2.2 
THE COURSE OF THE INTERVIEWS 
It is obvious from Table 6-5 Interrelation of pains and gains among members of project 
teamsTable 6-5 in chapter 0, showing the pains and gains for members from the different 
project teams, that the four respondents to the right have mentioned the most pains and 
gains. The reason for this is the division of the interview conduction in two rounds. The 
eight most left respondents (or nine, since the FMs comprise two individuals) represent the 
first round of interviews, where the goal was to, as openly as possible, gather information 
on all gains and pains among the respondents. The three most right respondents represent 
the comparative cases, that is, the second round of interviews. As explained in chapter 4.2.1 
Qualitative interviews,  Qualitative interviewsthe line of questioning for these three 
interviews had been adapted to increase the possibility for these respondents to be able to 
validate or deny findings from the first round of interviews.  
As for the fourth most right respondent in the table, PeS, almost the same reason 
applies. Since he represents the contractor in the Åbyhøjgård project, he belongs to the first 
round of respondents. However, for logistic reasons, his interview took place later than the 
rest of the interviews in both the first and second round. For this reason, he was asked the 
==PAGE:66==
Discussion 
[56] 
 
same modified questions as the respondents from the second round of interviews, leading 
to him mentioning more of the issues and values than the other respondents from the first 
round of interviews.  
All but three interviews have been conducted with one respondent per interview. 
The remaining three had two respondents present. These three were with Fl and JoM, the 
two facility managers at Åbyhøjgård, and Mo and Ti, respectively. This explains why Mo 
and Ti have mentioned the same values and issues in the “interrelations of interests” Table 
6-5. They are included in the table as two individuals nevertheless, since they have 
different areas of expertise, and thus complemented each other with statements on 
different topics. The views of the two facility managers are so alike that they share a column 
in Table 6-5. During the interview with MTH project manager Fl, the second respondent, 
JoM, only showed up for the last 15 minutes of the hour long interview. The appearance 
was unexpected by the interviewer, and consequently lead to some confusion and 
repetition of previously asked questions. The short time of presence by JoM naturally 
means that he has not been asked all of the questions, the other respondents have been 
asked. This is reflected in the “interrelations of interests” Table 6-5, where only one of the 
pains and gains in the table is ticked off in the column for JoM. Due to time constraints of 
the research and the busy schedule of JoM, a second interview was not conducted. 
7.2.2.3 
SELECTION OF RESIDENT RESPONDENTS 
A choice has been made to interview a rather large amount of residents compared to 
the other stakeholders. This is done to include as broad a range of individuals as possible. 
The sample of respondents among Åbyhøjgård residents does represent a wide range. 
Among respondents are senior citizens, young students, and middle-aged people, both in 
and out of the labor market. One respondent is on maternity leave. The respondents 
represent different ethnicities. Some have lived in the social housing departments since 
they were erected in 1968, some moved in 10-15 years ago, and others again moved in more 
recently, after the renovation was approved. Furthermore, residents are interviewed at 
different stages of the renovation, from having returned to their renovated apartment more 
than 6 months before the interview, to just having moved to the temporary relief housing. 
The only segment not represented among the residents is someone who voted 
against the renovation. This was not known prior to conducting the interviews, as the 
resident’s standpoint was not disclosed before in the beginning of each interview. A couple 
of the respondents did not vote at all, but all who did, voted in favor. However, they do 
not all agree on the scope chosen for the renovation, and they also have very different 
opinions on the course of the project so far. Yet admittedly, interviewing someone who 
voted against the renovation would have made the results even more reliable. 
7.2.2.4 
STAGE IN RENOVATION AT THE TIME OF THE INTERVIEW AFFECTS INTERVIEW ANSWERS 
A noteworthy discovery made through the qualitative interviews of resident 
respondents is the impact, their situation has on their answers. It is noted that the longer it 
has been since the renovation of their apartment was done, the more positive – or the less 
==PAGE:67==
Discussion 
[57] 
 
negative – their statements, and vice versa. Not only in the interviews, but also at the block 
and stairway meetings, organized for residents who are about to have their apartment 
renovated, a lot of concerns and complaints were expressed. 
The observed change in behavior makes sense. It is logical that people are relieved 
when the renovation of their home is finished, and tend to neglect the bad experiences they 
may have had during the renovation. For this reason, evaluations of the renovation process 
that take place during the renovation will probably reveal different concerns than 
evaluations organized after the renovation is completed. The most suitable time for 
evaluating therefore depends on the aim of the evaluation. 
7.2.2.5 
SELECTION OF PROJECT TEAM RESPONDENTS 
The respondents from the Åbyhøjgård project team have been chosen based on their 
everyday connection to the project. All but two respondents work on site, and hence they 
are believed to be best equipped to assess the construction process of the renovation 
project. The remaining two (the director of Al2bolig, Al, and the head of section of MTH, 
PeS), though not involved in the everyday process, have been interviewed do to their 
significant impact on the decision making in the project. Only representatives from the 
housing organizations managing the two comparative cases have been interviewed, due 
to time constraints.   
Had more time been available, it would have been interesting to interview 
representatives from the consultant companies, who are involved in the projects, yet not 
working on site. Other interesting stakeholders, who could have perhaps contributed to 
further findings, are the subcontractors and the craftsmen.  
7.2.2.6 
SELECTION OF CASES 
The renovation of Åbyhøjgård is called a “general plan” by the social housing 
organization, so it should actually have been excluded as a subject for this research, 
according to the criteria in Table 4-2. However, because the term “general plan” in 
connection to Åbyhøjgård is not used in the context explained in Table 4-2, but rather to 
emphasize the great physical scope of the renovation, the project is not contradicting the 
criteria for case selection, and is still suitable for this research. 
Conversely, another argument could be made against the suitability of Åbyhøjgård 
as main case for this research: According to the experience of the three expert respondents 
representing the two secondary cases, it is not common to use a turnkey contract for 
sustainable renovation projects similar to Åbyhøjgård. This could be argued as a limitation 
of the generalization factor of the case. On the other hand, only few of the found gains and 
pains among the stakeholders are connected to the type of contract, so the impact on the 
results is assessed to be insignificant. 
The second criteria for trustworthiness, as explained in chapter 4.2.3, “the 
transferability of the results”, is considered fulfilled in this research by verifying the results 
through respondents from two cases besides the main case. The trustworthiness could be 
==PAGE:68==
Discussion 
[58] 
 
further increased by conducting this research on other sustainable renovations of social 
housing dwellings under different conditions, e.g. situated in other parts of Denmark, with 
different sizes of housing departments, with different compositions of residents etc. 
7.2.2.7 
WOULD A DIFFERENT CASE HAVE PROVIDED DIFFERENT GAINS AND PAINS? 
It cannot be ruled out that a different case in terms of a different social housing 
renovation project would have revealed further gains and pains, but maybe it would also 
have overlooked some of the gains and pains found in this study. As suggested above, the 
best way to investigate this and increase the generalization factor is to conduct the same 
research on more renovation projects. 
 SUITABILITY OF CASE STUDY RESEARCH TO BUILD THEORY FROM 
As stated in chapter 4.1 Case study research, some believe that case study research is 
only preliminary and cannot be used to test propositions and build theory. Especially the 
issue of generalization is being contested. In his 2006 article, Flyvbjerg cites the Dictionary 
of Sociology, where the term “Case study” is described as “The detailed examination of a 
single example of a class of phenomena, a case study cannot provide reliable information about the 
broader class, but it may be useful in the preliminary stages of an investigation since it provides 
hypotheses, which may be tested systematically with a larger number of cases. (Abercrombie, Hill, 
& Turner, 1984, p. 34)” (Flyvbjerg 2006, p. 220). Through thorough argumentation, 
Flyvbjerg challenges this view and arrives at the conclusion that “One can often generalize 
on the basis of a single case, and the case study may  be central to scientific development via 
generalization as supplement or alternative to other methods.” (Flyvbjerg 2006, p. 228).  
Eisenhardt’s widely cited article from 1989 describes several strengths of using case study 
research to build theory, e.g. “[…] the resultant theory is likely to be empirically valid. The 
likelihood of valid theory is high because the theory-building process is so intimately tied with 
evidence […]. This intimate interaction with actual evidence often produces theory which closely 
mirrors reality.” (Eisenhardt 1989, p. 547). Additional to this argument and the reasons 
stated in chapter 4.1, the following statement by Eisenhardt further substantiates the use 
of case study research in this thesis. She states “[…] building theory from case study research 
is most appropriate in the early stages of research on a topic or to provide freshness in perspective to 
an already researched topic.” (Eisenhardt 1989, p. 548). As indicated in chapter 3 Research 
questions, this research is the “early stage” of research on this topic. Future, further 
research can include implementing the findings of this thesis in renovation projects, and 
subsequently analyzing the results of implementation. Moreover, this research provides a 
“freshness in perspective” by looking at the soft concept of value creation instead of the 
classic “time, quality, money”-criteria of success of a project. 
 POOR FEEDBACK ON SURVEY 
As stated in chapter 4.2.2 Survey, the process of collecting survey answers has been 
iterative.  
==PAGE:69==
Discussion 
[59] 
 
The method used in the first round of survey data collection of personally visiting 
residents in Åbyhøjgård, was chosen to be able to evaluate the residents’ understanding of 
the survey questions.  Although the survey was tested on a few outsider respondents 
beforehand, these outsider respondents all have experience in answering surveys of this 
kind, which not all resident respondents could be expected to have. Thus, the approval 
from the outsider respondents did not provide a guarantee that the actual respondents 
would understand the questions. After collecting eight answers from Åbyhøjgård residents 
in the first round, it was clear that there were in fact some misunderstandings, and a 
decision was made to interrupt the data collection. The subsequent modification of the 
survey template has made the initial answers partly unusable. 
To be able to reach a larger volume of resident respondents with the modified survey 
template in shorter time, it has been created online. This way it is not possible to spot 
misunderstandings, so the modified explanations of the questions have to be trusted to be 
sufficient. Another drawback is that an online survey prevents some segments of residents 
from participating, e.g. elderly people or others with no computer, and illiterates. Even so, 
the benefits of an online survey have been assessed to outweigh the drawbacks.  
In an attempt to ensure a high response rate on the online survey, possible resident 
respondents from the Åbyhøjgård project have been contacted by phone prior to receiving 
the survey by email. Though residents were generally positive and expressed a wish to 
contribute to the project by answering the survey, the response rate among the residents 
who received a phone call was less than 30%. Besides sending out the survey on emails, it 
has been posted on a Facebook page for the residents of the secondary case, Rosenhøj, 
called Nyt Rosenhøj. Around 400 people follow this page. Unfortunately, despite these 
varied attempts and a total potential feedback size of around 430 people, only 12 answers 
have been collected among residents, making the results too inconclusive to use for 
generalizing – even when including the usable parts of the eight responses on the initial 
survey template. 
The survey among project team respondents was sent out after having been modified 
based on the misunderstandings among residents in the first round of survey data 
collection. This template was also created online and sent by email to 10 of the 13 interview 
respondents from the three project teams. Due to time constraints it was not distributed to 
further respondents, and also not to the three remaining interview respondents, whose 
email addresses were not at hand. Seven of the 10 project team members answered the 
survey. While this is a response rate of 70%, the seven answers are not enough to base 
conclusions on. 
Because of the small amount of responses it cannot be concluded with certainty that 
the survey templates developed for this research work as intended. However, conducting 
this kind of survey before initiating future sustainable renovations is believed to be very 
useful, in order to reveal the importance of different aspects of the process, and thus ensure 
maximized value creation in the process. The survey templates enclosed in appendix A5 
==PAGE:70==
Discussion 
[60] 
 
are assumed to be a good starting point. To ensure a proper response rate, it is suggested 
to hand out and collect the surveys in person, and to orally explain the relevance and intent 
of the survey to the respondents. 
7.3 CONTRIBUTION TO THEORY 
This research las led to two additional, theoretical findings. One is the use of the VPC 
in a new context, where the “customers” are all the different stakeholders in a construction 
project. The other is the result of combining the VPC and SNA.  
The SNA tool Social Network Mapping can be used in combination with the VPC to 
map agreements and alliances among stakeholders, as seen in Figure 6-3, page 35. This 
combination enables the Social Network Map to also deal with the context of exchanged 
information, not only the presence or absence of links. Again, it should be noted that the 
network only illustrates which pains and gains were mentioned by each respondent during 
the interviews, and that this does not necessarily equal that the shown topics are the only 
ones, the respondents see as important. This limitation can be mitigated in future use by 
basing the network on survey answers for future projects. With this approach it will be 
possible to include the degree of importance (for survey questions answered with the 
Likert scale) in the weight of the lines between nodes, which will affect the size of the node, 
and thereby give a more nuanced picture of the most important aspects to the stakeholders. 
Yet, the strength of the network shown in Figure 6-3 on page 35 is that it shows 
people’s immediate thoughts and opinions instead of forcing them to have an opinion on 
every topic, which is a risk of using survey answers. 
 
 
==PAGE:71==
Conclusion 
[61] 
 
8 CONCLUSION 
The first question, this research sought to answer, is: 
 
Who are the primary stakeholders in sustainable renovation projects of social 
housing? 
o How are their internal dynamics? 
It has been established that there are six primary stakeholders in the main case, the 
sustainable renovation project of the social housing department Åbyhøjgård, and hereof 
four key players. The four key players are the residents (including the board of 
department), the social housing organization, the turn-key contractor and the consultants. 
The two other primary stakeholders, who are not seen as key players, are the DNBF and 
the sub-contractors.  
Based on this finding, 20 people have been chosen as interview respondents for the 
thesis, representing all the key players except for the consultants. From the interview 
answers, a Social Network Map has been created, which reveals the internal dynamics 
between the stakeholders. The map has revealed a well-integrated network of 
communication. 
The second question, this research sought to answer, is: 
 
What matters to the primary stakeholders in the renovation process? 
o Which aspects do stakeholders gain from? 
o Which aspects stand in the way of value creation, i.e. which aspects do 
stakeholders see as pains? 
A total of 21 aspects that matter to the inquired stakeholders have been identified, 
hereof 11 gains and 10 pains. They are listed in the table below. 
Gains 
Pains

Too many errors and omissions, and 
too slow correction process 
To have someone to ask questions about the 
renovation

renovation and time schedule) not

by the housing organization and 
contractor, being given a low priority in 
the project

housing organization/contractor

leaving belongings in apartments

result 
 
Table 8-2 Gains and pains identified by respondents – continued from previous page 
The third and final question, this research sought to answer, is: 
 
Do agreements on important topics exist between stakeholders? 
o If yes, how are the interests in topics interrelated among stakeholders? 
The answer is that agreements do exist on most topics. However, the research shows 
that the degree of agreement varies widely, depending on the topic.  
In an attempt to determine the degree of importance of each of the found gains and 
pains to each stakeholder, and hereby also establish a more prominent distinction of the 
alliances of interests, a survey template has been developed. The survey was conducted 
among both residents and key members of project teams, but, unfortunately, the response 
rate has been too low to be able to draw conclusions from.  
Still, it is considered to be very useful to conduct this type of survey before initiating 
future sustainable renovations, and the templates developed through this research are 
believed to be suitable for this purpose. 
Additional to answering the research questions, this research has led to two 
contributions to current theory. One is the use of the Value Proposition Canvas in a new 
context, where the “customers” are all the different stakeholders in a construction project. 
The other is the use of the Value Proposition Canvas in combination with Social Network 
Analysis. This combination enables the Social Network Map to also deal with the context 
of exchanged information, not only the presence or absence of links.

A1 CASE FACTS 
This appendix includes all relevant facts about the primary case; the renovation of 
Åbyhøjgård, as well as about the two secondary cases; Gyvelparken and Rosenhøj. The information 
has been collected through interviews, project web sites, and documents provided by residents and 
other stakeholders. 
PRIMARY CASE: ÅBYHØJGÅRD 
The main case in this study is the social housing departments Åbyhøjgård, which comprise 
the two departments; no. 110 and no. 310, under the social housing organization Al2bolig. Al2bolig 
administers around 4,500 dwellings in the Aarhus area, 297 of which are located in Åbyhøjgård. 
Åbyhøjgård consists of ten blocks of apartments of different sizes, with 1 to 4 bedrooms. 
The renovation of Åbyhøjgård is extensive, and can be divided in outdoor and indoor works. 
The complete scope of the renovation is shown in Table 1 below. 
Åbyhøjgård – Outdoor works 
 
New, energy efficient windows and cladding elements on both front and back façades 
 
Improved insulation and new, energy efficient oriel windows on end walls 
 
Correction of roof 
 
Replacement of doors and windows in hallways 
 
Replacement of basement windows 
 
Piling 
Åbyhøjgård – Indoor works 
 
Replacement of toilet waste pipes 
 
Replacement of pipes in bathroom floor and of floor tiles 
 
New ventilation system with heat recovery and range hood function 
 
Improved sealing of walls between apartments to prevent smell and noise nuisances  
 
New windowsills in front façades 
 
Replacement of elements between living room and balcony 
 
Replacement of entrance doors 
 
New handrails on staircases 
Table 1 Scope of renovation of Åbyhøjgård 
The indoor renovation described in Table 1 only applies to seven of the ten apartment blocks. 
The remaining three blocks, 81 apartments, are turned into accessible dwellings (class C), which 
==PAGE:78==
A1 Case facts 
2 
 
means they are completely renovated inside with new kitchens and bathrooms, elevators, a uniform 
level floor, and no thresholds anywhere in the buildings, making them wheel chair accessible. 
The renovation is partly funded through increases in rent. To cover the remaining expenses, 
Al2bolig receives financial support from the Danish National Building Fund (abbrev. DNBF). The 
obtainable support to establish accessibility dwellings is significantly higher than to other 
renovation actions. This means that the overall rent increase for residents is lower if the renovation 
includes comprehensive remodeling of some dwellings into accessibility housing, compared to 
‘only’ carrying out an energy renovation. 
The project has been tendered as a turnkey contract, and the bidding was won by MT Højgaard 
(abbrev. MTH). The renovation is carried out in different stages, block by block. Outdoor tasks are 
initiated first as they comprise the most comprehensive work. Then work begins in the halls and 
stairways. There are three to four stairways in each block. Finally, indoor works are carried out. 
When the craftsmen begin working inside the apartments, the residents move to a temporary relief 
housing facility placed on site. They leave most of their furniture in the apartments, piled up in 
assigned places, and shielded or wrapped in plastic. The residents have access to their apartments 
during the entire renovation process, and are advised by Al2bolig to check up on their things, e.g. 
that the refrigerator is still running. The residents are moved to the temporary housing facilities one 
stairway at a time, and stay there for eight weeks before they return to the renovated apartment. 
Residents from one stairway are moved in and out of the temporary housing every other week, so 
on average, indoor work is carried out in four stairways at a time.  
The schedule is different for the three apartment blocks being turned into accessible housing. 
Since this renovation is far more comprehensive, residents here will live in the temporary housing 
facilities for six months. During this time, their furniture will be stored in a Self-Storage located not 
far from Åbyhøjgård. 
The renovation began in September 2015, and the first indoor works were initiated in 
December 2015. According to the project schedule, the seven blocks not being turned into accessible 
housing will be completed in January 2017. In December 2016, outdoor works on the first accessible 
housing block has begun, and the first stairway of residents moved to the temporary housing in 
January 2017. The entire renovation project is expected to be completed in July 2018. 
SECONDARY CASES: GYVELPARKEN AND ROSENHØJ 
 One of the two secondary cases, used as comparison and for validation of data gathered from 
Åbyhøjgård, is the social housing department Gyvelparken, which is administered by the social 
housing organization ALBOA. ALBOA manages around 7,100 dwellings distributed on 75 
departments in the Aarhus area. Gyvelparken consists of 69 apartments with 1, 2, or 4 bedrooms, 
placed in two story row houses. Residents live in their apartments during the renovation, but since 
the water is cut off, mobile kitchens and bathrooms are installed outside for the residents to use. 
Other facts have been stated in chapter 4.2.4 Case selection. Since the case of the renovation of 
Gyvelparken has been used as a starting point for a general conversation with the representative 
from ALBOA, rather than for a concrete case comparison, it will not be described further. 
==PAGE:79==
A1 Case facts 
3 
 
The renovation of Rosenhøj is the second of the two comparative cases. Rosenhøj is one of 17 
departments administered by the social housing organization Århus Omegn. There are around 3,000 
dwellings in the 17 departments in total, and 808 of them are located in Rosenhøj. Most of the 808 
dwellings are located in four story apartment blocks, 22 are placed in row houses, which have been 
erected as part of the renovation project. During the renovation, 191 dwellings have been turned 
into accessible dwellings.  
The renovation of Rosenhøj is part of a comprehensive general plan for the area Viby Syd. The 
project was tendered in two multi-trade contracts, one for indoor works, and one for outdoor works. 
The scope of the renovation is similar to the one of Åbyhøjgård, except it is in a larger scale, and the 
renovation of the outdoor areas is more comprehensive. Besides the renovation, the Rosenhøj project 
includes the abovementioned construction of row houses, as well as a new facility management 
building. 
Rosenhøj covers a large area of ground. Thus, it has been possible to divide the renovation 
process in stages, covering different physical parts of the area at a time. This has been done to avoid 
a feeling among the residents of living on a construction site during the entire project, which lasts 
around three years.

A2 INTERVIEW GUIDE 
QUESTIONS FOR RESIDENT RESPONDENTS 
 
Hvor længe har du boet i afdelingen? I hvilken størrelse bolig? 
 
Hvilken bolig kommer du/er du kommet tilbage til? 
 
Er du, eller har du været, en del af afdelingsbestyrelsen eller andre funktioner i 
beboerdemokratiet? 
 
Hvorfor var du for/imod projektet? 
 
Hvornår blev du første gang involveret i projektet? 
 
I hvilke sammenhænge har du været involveret i projektet: 
o I idefasen? 
o I planlægningsfasen? 
o Under udførelsen? 
o Efter udførelsen? 
 
Hvilken indflydelse har du haft på projektet?/Hvad har du bidraget med til projektet? 
o Har du udnyttet den indflydelse, du har haft mulighed for at have? 
o Hvis projektet kunne laves om, ville du så have gjort noget anderledes? 
 
Hvad forventer du af projektet? (at arbejdet lever op til kvalitetskrav, at tidsplanen 
overholdes, at der gøres noget ekstra fx socialt, at der bliver lyttet og reageret på 
henvendelser/indsigelser, samarbejdsvillighed og positivitet etc.) 
 
Hvad kunne gøre dit liv lettere i forhold til projektet? Hvad kunne lette processen for dig? 
 
Hvad er succeskriteriet(r) for dig i forhold til projektet? (kvalitet i top, overholde tidsplan, 
nye sociale relationer/tiltag etc.) 
 
Hvilke bekymringer har du (haft) i forbindelse med renoveringsprojektet? (Hvad frygter 
du?) 
 
Hvad er de største udfordringer i projektet for dig? 
 
Synes du, der er blevet taget hånd om dine forventninger/bekymringer i processen? (Hvem 
har taget hånd om dem? Var det dem, du synes, skulle tage hånd om dem?) 
o Før udførelsen: 
 
Hvordan? 
 
Hvorfor ikke? 
o Under udførelsen: 
 
Hvordan? 
 
Hvorfor ikke? 
 
Er der noget af større betydning, som ikke er gået/blevet, som du havde regnet med? 
o Positivt: 
o Negativt: 
 
Har din opfattelse af projektet ændret sig (i positiv eller negativ retning) siden projektets 
godkendelse? Hvad skyldes din holdningsændring? 
Informationsflow: 
 
Hvilke folk i projektet er du i kontakt med?

A2 Interview guide 
5 
 
o Hvor ofte er du i kontakt med dem hver især? 
o Hvem er den vigtigste interessent for dig? Hvorfor? 
o Hvem går du til med spørgsmål/Hvilke informationer får du fra hvem? (Fx om 
tidsplan, projektets indhold, varslinger, arbejder i din bolig, fejl og mangler, 
genhusning etc.) 
 
Er de informationer, du får, tilfredsstillende? 
o Hvilke informationer giver du til hvem? 
 
Har du gjort brug af 110310renovering.dk eller andre digitale muligheder for at få/give 
informationer? 
Overordnet: 
 
Er dine forventninger blevet indfriet (ind til videre)?/Regner du med, at dine forventninger 
bliver indfriet? 
 
Hvis projektet kunne gøres om, ville du så have gjort noget anderledes?  
Evt: 
 
Hvad synes du om genhusningsordningen?

o Negativt:



o Hvornår og angående hvad har beboerne været involveret (faser, typer af møder)? 
o Hvornår har driftsfolk været involveret? 
o Er det blevet tydeliggjort, hvor og hvornår beboerne har haft reel indflydelse,

o Hvad er blevet gjort for at forberede brugerne på resultatet af projektet/forberede 
driftspersonalet? 
 
Er der andre end beboerne i afdelingen, der bruger børneinstitutionen på afdelingens 
område?  
o Hvis ja, (hvornår) er de så blevet hørt i processen? (fx om adgangsforhold under 
udførelsen) 
 
Har I planer om at evaluere og samle erfaringer fra beboerne (og andre interessenter) ved

 
Hvis projektet kunne gøres om, ville du så have gjort noget anderledes?

QUESTIONS FOR COMPARATIVE HOUSING ASSOCIATIONS 
 
Hvor længe har du været hos boligorganisationen? 
 
Hvordan opstod ideen om en renovering? Hvem foreslog den i første omgang? 
 
Hvilken entrepriseform bruger I til renoveringer? 
 
Hvad forventer du af projektet? (at arbejdet lever op til kvalitetskrav, at tidsplanen 
overholdes, at der gøres noget ekstra fx socialt, at der bliver lyttet og reageret på 
henvendelser/indsigelser, samarbejdsvillighed og positivitet etc.) 
 
Hvilke problemer har I mødt under renoveringen?/Er der noget af større betydning, som 
ikke er gået som planlagt? 
 
Hvad forventer du af processen i et renoveringsprojekt? (at arbejdet lever op til 
kvalitetskrav, at tidsplanen overholdes, at der gøres noget ekstra fx socialt, at der bliver 
lyttet og reageret på henvendelser/indsigelser, samarbejdsvillighed og positivitet etc.) 
 
Hvad er succeskriteriet(r) for dig i forhold til projektet? (at resultatet bliver godt, kvalitet i 
top, overholde tidsplan/budget etc.) 

